CURRENCY EQUIVALENTS
In October 1975, the Kenya Shilling was devalued and pegged to
the SDR at a rate of SDR 1.00 = KShs. 9.66, and the rate vis-a-vis the
US Dollar has fluctuated since that time. For purposes of this appraisal
the following exchange rate has been used:
US$1.00 = Kenya Shilling (KShs.) 8.00
KShs. 1.00 = US$0.12

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECr
Existing Structure and Organization of the Sugar Industry
A. Introduction
1.        Government plays an important role in Kenya's sugar industry.
Various ministries and other agencies are involved in production development
schemes, price and cost regulatory functions and marketing; there is, however,
a relatively low level of coordination between these institutions. The sugar
companies have various mixes of Government and private ownership and manage-
ment systems, particularly with respect to outgrower cane production. With an
increasing emphasis on smallholder cane production, the industry is faced with
the difficult task of finding appropriate strategies and organizational
arrangements for increasing cane production and at the same time developing
efficient smallholder farm enterprise.
B. Institutions Concerned With the Industry
The Ministry of Agriculture
2.        The Ministry has overall responsibility for policy formulation
and the development of the industry. Government has major share capital
investments in five of the seven sugar companies (Mumias, Muhoroni, Chemelil,
Nzoia, South Nyanza) and a Ministry representative sits on the Board of
Directors of each of these companies. There are two technical divisions in
the Ministry of Agriculture, Crops and Extension/Training. A Director of
Agriculture in each province supervises the District Agricultural Officers who
direct the extension work. Only minimal extension services are available
directly to cane producers from the Ministry, although some 25 general agri-
culture extension agents have been made available to the Sugar Belt Coopera-
tive Union (SBCU). The Ministry's National Sugar Research Station 1/ at Kibos
was established in 1971 on the western end of the Sugar Belt; most of the
efforts at Kibos have been devoted to variety trials, with some work on
fertilizer rates, soil improvement, herbicides, ratoon-stunting disease, and
rate of application of N. The paucity of qualified research personnel has
been a serious constraint on the quality of work.
1/   The name is being changed and the new name, the National Sugar Research
Institute, is used throughout this report.
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance
of their official duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
The Ministry of Finance and Economic Planning (The Treasury)
3.        This Ministry plays a central role in determining policy for sugar
industry development, particularly with respect to Government expenditures
and foreign exchange matters. It also participates in the determination
of sugar prices, taxes on sugar production and levies on sugar imports.
The Ministry of Commerce and Industry
4.        This Ministry is responsible for establishing sugar marketing
policy and for control and direction of the sales and distribution functions.
It also imports sugar as required to supplement internal production. The
Ministry directs the Kenya National Trading Company (KNTC) in the wholesale
distribution of sugar (Annex 2).
The Ministry of Lands and Settlement
5.        The Land Department of this Ministry is responsible for land sur-
veys, adjudication and registration of titles and other rights. It is also
responsible for acquisition of land for Government sponsored housing and
industrial development and for acquisition of large farms for settlement
schemes for landless small farmers. The Settlement Department administers
the sub-division and redistribution of the land and is responsible for
development of infrastructure and, in some cases, for agricultural development
(Annex 3).
The Ministry of Cooperative Development
6.        This Ministry is responsible for administering a wide range of
programs and for enforcing the Cooperative Law, registration of societies
and unions, auditing of accounts, training in cooperative management and
bookkeeping, and general supervision and guidance. The Ministry also
operates the Cooperative College of Kenya. Important recent activities
are the Ministry's Cooperative Production Credit Scheme (CPCS), financed
by the Cooperative Bank of Kenya (para. 15) and the Cooperative Thrift
Scheme (CTS), a savings program for small farmers. Cooperatives have been
vital in developing small-scale sugarcane production in the Nyanza Sugar
Belt (Annex 3).
The National Irrigation Board (NIB)
7.        The NIB is a quasi-governmental body responsible for development
of large-scale irrigation projects. Although no cane is currently produced
on any of the large-scale irrigation projects, the NIB pilot project on
the Kano plain in the Sugar Belt has about 200 ha of cane in production
and 30 ha have been converted from rice to sugarcane at Ahero.
The Ministry of Water Development
8.        This Ministry is responsible for development of large-scale
domestic water supply and distribution systems for urban areas and for



-3-
ANNEX 1
Page 3
rural domestic consumption. It also develops small-scale irrigation projects.
The hydrology and groundwater studies and general planning for water resources
are done in this Ministry.
The Ministry of Works
9.        This Ministry has responsibilities for planning, construction, and
maintenance of public roads and buildings. This function extends to main
access roads in the cane growing areas.
Banking Institutions
10.       The private banks in Kenya lend about 12% of total commercial
credit to the agriculture sector. The bulk of the financing for agriculture,
however, comes from four Government institutions.
11.       Agricultural Development Corporation (ADC).  Financed by grants
and loans from the Ministry of Agriculture, this Government-owned development
bank has made investments in Chemelil Sugar Company, Ltd., amounting to
KShs. 151 million as of March 31, 1976, of which KShs. 94.4 million is equity
and KShs. 56.6 million is in loans. ADC's investment in East African Sugar
Industries, Ltd. amounts to KShs. 19.5 million of equity capital.
12.       Agricultural Finance Corporation (AFC).  Also Government-owned,
AFC has no equity investments in the sugar industry but is a source of
financing for some outgrowers. Land is the collateral but loans generally
exceed the value of the land. Owing to the relative ease of administering
loans to large farmers as opposed to small farmers, the AFC's lending
operations tend to be primarily to large farms. However, the AFC has made
a special effort in the Ramisi area, where no other credit is available to
small-scale growers, to finance their operations.
13.       Development Finance Company of Kenya Limited (DFCK).  This develop-
ment finance company is owned partly by Government through the Industrial and
Commercial Development Corporation (ICDC). The other shareholders with ICDC
are the Commonwealth Development Corporation, German Development Company,
Ltd., and the Netherlands Finance Company for Developing Countries, Ltd.
DFCK invests in new or expanding manufacturing and agricultural processing
enterprises and has made equity investments in Chemelil Sugar Company.
14.       The Cooperative Bank of Kenya, Ltd. (CBK).  The CBK, starting oper-
ations in 1968, is the banker of the cooperative societies and unions. In
addition to Government funds, CBK receives financing from bilateral and
multilateral assistance and finance organizations. Through the Cooperative
Thrift Scheme (CTS), a member savings program, additional funds are generated
within the cooperative system for lending. With recent additional financing
from USAID and a US$20 million loan from the World Bank, CBK now claims to
have over KShs. 300 million for lending. CBK has outstanding loans of over
KShs. 80 million to nearly 100,000 farmers.



4-
ANNEX 1
Page 4
15.       The Cooperative Production Credit Scheme (CPCS) is the principal
lending mechanism of CBK whereby members borrow through their societies
and unions for production inputs. The CPCS lending program of the Sugar
Belt Cooperative Union finances private cane grown by marketing society
members and the private cane grown on subsistence plots by production society
members. The member applies to the society for a production loan to cover
costs of land preparation, inputs and crop maintenance. The society loan
committee and a CPCS field agent review the application, check the plot
for size and suitability and, if approved, the loan is submitted to the
CPCS office in Chemelil. The society supervises and certifies performance.
The loan is recovered for CPCS by the factory at the time of cane delivery.
Loan repayments to date have been good, but the program is of too recent
origin to draw any definite conclusions.
C. Industry Organization at the National Level
The Kenya Sugar Authority (KSA)
16.       The Kenya Sugar Authority is the principal Government body respon-
sible for development and coordination in the industry. KSA was created
in 1972 as a statutory board "to promote the development of the sugar industry
in the most efficient and economical manner possible, and in particular to
promote and foster practices which enhance the efficiency of the production,
processing and marketing of sugarcane, sugar in all its forms and molasses."
KSA has remained an ineffective advisory body until now. However, its powers,
staff and operations are currently being expanded with funds from the South
Nyanza Sugar Project, which provides US$2.6 million for KSA over a period of
five years starting in 1977.
17.       The-overall responsibility for the management of the Authority is
vested jointly in the members of the Authority in accordance with the Agri-
culture Act (Cap 318), Kenya Sugar Authority (Amendment) Order 1977. In
addition to the members of the Authority, headed by a Chairman, the KSA
organization consists of a Chief Executive and expert staff in the areas of
planning, finance, economics, and evaluation; sugarcane production; factory
technology; and manpower development. KSA's objectives and functions are
described in Kenya: Appraisal of the South Nyanza Sugar Project, Report No.
1418-KE of March 4, 1977, Annex 17.
East Africa Storage Company
18.       This company, affiliated with the international molasses marketing
company United Molasses Inc., handles all of the molasses exports from Kenya.
With a storage facility in Mombasa of 21,000 t and an annual capacity of
120,000 t, it is well-equipped to handle the available molasses. The company
expects that a new Government ruling which allows private companies to own
rolling stock on the railroad will eventually relieve the serious transporta-
tion problem which now exists in moving molasses from the factories to the
port of Mombasa.

ANNEX 1

D. Organization at the Company Level
19.       There are seven sugar production companies in Kenya (five operat-
ing and two with factories under construction), plus two firms operating
mini-factories. Detailed features of organization and management of the
Project companies at Miwani, Chemelil, Muhoroni and Ramisi are described in
Annex 3. The main features of the other companies are briefly summarized
below.
20.       The Mumias Sugar Company, Ltd., formed in 1971, is managed under
contract by Bookers Agricultural and Technical Service, Ltd. The majority
equity in Mumias is held by Government. The cane supply of 548,000 t in 1976
was provided by the nucleus estate (31%) and small outgrowers (69%). Mumias
is in an expansion stage which will take the annual capacity of the factory
from 70,000 t sugar to 156,000 t and eventually to 180,000 t. Mumias has
formed an outgrowers company to handle financing of outgrower production and
eventually, if its performance is encouraging, to organize and manage the
agriculture operations, harvesting and transport which are all now done by the
factory. Farmers are represented on the board of directors of the outgrowers
company.
21.       The South Nyanza Sugar Company, Ltd., formed in 1976, is expected
to commission its new factory in 1979/80 and is being established with major-
ity equity from Government (95%). Loan capital is being provided by the World
Bank and other institutions. The Mehta Group, which also manages Muhoroni,
are the managing agents. The development includes a 2,650 ha nucleus estate
and 9,000 ha of land for small-scale outgrower production and will increase
sugar production by 60,000 t per year.
22.       The Nzoia Sugar Company, Ltd. is a Government-owned, French financed
sugar company now constructing a factory and developing cane in Western Prov-
ince. Nzoia will have a capacity of 52,000 t of sugar per year and plans to
develop about 12,000 ha of cane. Commissioning of the factory is expected in
late 1978.
23.       There are two privately-owned mini-factories in Kenya, one open-pan
system at Kabras in Western Province produces about 1,800 t of sugar per year,
and a new factory with a vacuum system is currently under construction also in
Western Province. Possibilities for further large-scale development exist at
Busia, the Yala Swamp and other areas but Government has earlier agreed to
discuss with the Bank any further significant expansion of the sugar industry.
May 30, 1978

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
The Project Area
1.        The Project would be carried at two localities (Maps IBRD 12990 and
IBRD 12997), separated by some 650 km in a straight line from Muhoroni to
Ramisi, or more than 800 km road distance between those places. In the west
of Kenya the Nyanza Sugar Belt lies between Kisumu on Lake Victoria and the
Tinderet-Lumbwa mountainous tract. In the far south of the country, Ramisi
Sugar Zone occupies the coastal plain beneath the Shimba Hills.
Nyanza Sugar Belt
2.        Location.  Each of the sugar mills at Miwani, Chemelil and
Muhoroni is served by a surrounding estate and by outgrowers of sugarcane
whose farms extend up to 25-28 km by road from the factory. The three
adjacent blocks of land, referred to2as zones in Kenya, which supply the
mills cover altogether some 1,000 km of the Kano plain and its fringes to
the north, as far as the cliffs of the Nandi Escarpment, and to the east
where the Tinderet Hills and the uplands beyond Fort Ternan mark the limits
of the Sugar Belt. The zones straddle the equator, having a maximum width
of more than 30 km between latitudes 00 05'N and 0014'S and a maximum length
of about 70 km between 34046'E, the longtitude of Kisumu, and 35 022'E. Dis-
tances by road from Kisumu are Miwani 28 km, Chemelil 47 km, Muhoroni 56 km,
and thence, via Kericho, Nairobi 354 km. The Sugar Belt falls almost entirely
in the Kisumu District of Nyanza Province, except along its south and east
margins (Kericho District) and its northern rim (Nandi District). Kericho
and Nandi Districts are in Rift Valley Province.
3.        History.  The irregular shapes of these zone result from the un-
even spread of sugarcane growing around the mills. Before 1922, when Miwani
factory was built, the area had been mainly part of the domain of the Luo
people, with some territory belonging to the Nandi in the north and to the
Kipsigis in the south. The eastern end of the present Nyanza Sugar Belt is
its latest accretion but not the only growth point, for within 10-15 km road
distance from Miwani there is still land awaiting development or newly
developed - notably Ahero Irrigation Scheme.
4.        Demography.  According to the 1969 population census Kisumu
District then had 401,000 inhabitants. The town of Kisumu accounted for
31,000 and the remaining rural area, the Nyanza Sugar Belt and2environs
(2,080 km ), therefore, had a population density of 178 per km . The
predicted 1979 population of the District, minus Kisumu town, is 535,000
at 3.75% annual growth rate, giving a forecast 1979 density of some 260 per



ANNEX 2
Page 2
2
km .  There is uneven distribution of people in the various census sub-
locations of the Sugar Belt. Some sublocations had almost twice the mean
density in 1969, led by the smallholder cane-growLng areas south and south-
west of Miwani mill (280-350 per km ). In contrait Fort Ternan sublocation
was then relatively sparsely inhabited (50 per km ) as were the nucleus
estate areas around Miwani and Chemelil mills. Currently, there are some
14,000 farmers in the Nyanza Sugar Belt, most of whom grow cane, and some
3,000 permanent staff of the sugar companies and of other major employers
within the sugar zones. The total population of all three zones is not easy
to estimate, but at six dependents per farmer or employee,2the 1977 total of
some 100,000 would entail an overall density of 100 per km . Rural Kisumu
District outside the Suga5 Belt would on this reckoning have a present average
density of 300-350 per km .
5.        Land Tenure.  The Sugar Belt, with about 75% of the land under cul-
tivation, is characterized by two distinct groupings of farmers. These are
medium to large farmers with holdings ranging from 25 to 2,000 ha and the
small farmers with an average holding of about four hectares. The large farms
were owned by European and other immigrant cultivators before independence;
very few Europeans remain at present. Some Asian farmers also have been sell-
ing their farms. Although traditional villages have existed in the area for
a long time, the majority of the small farms have been created since independ-
ence. Land adjudication has been completed on about 50% of the land and is
expected to be completed for the whole area in the next three years.
6.        Type of Farming.  The Sugar Belt is traditionally a diversified,
subsistence crop area with considerable grazing in the poorly drained, rocky
and steep areas. Cane had been grown by large estates for over 50 years
and on smaller farms for about a decade. Sisal was also once a prominent
cash crop. Principal subsistence crops include maize, millet, cassava,
beans, and sweet potatoes. Cultivation is mostly done by hand tools in sub-
sistence farming. Part-time farming is common as there exist ample employ-
ment opportunities in nearby Kisumu town and witb the sugar companies.
7.        Communications.  Since the turn of the century the rail link Kisumu-
Nairobi-Mombasa has played a crucial role in the economy of Nyanza Province.
Sugar mills were deliberately sited close to the line. The railway traverses
the Sugar Belt, rather more cane land being north of the track than south,
mainly because there are extensive swamps and places with impermeable soil to
the south. The Sugar Belt's tarred road network is of recent origin, compri-
sing loops and spurs from the main B-1 highway from Kisumu via Kericho to
Nairobi. There are small airstrips near each sugar mill and scheduled internal
services from Kisumu Airport.
8.        Factory Zones and Rezoning Proposals.  Since 1966 there has been
mutually agreed geographic demarcation between suppliers of cane to each
of the mills whose zones are adjacent - and indeed would overlap if economic
distance of transportation were the sole criterion for drawing zone limits.
There have been occassional revisions of zonal boundaries and their position
as of May 1977 is shown on an inset, Map IBRD 12988. Rezoning as proposed by



ANNEX 2
Page 3
the appraisal mission and already implemented (Legal Notice No. 227 of
August 19, 1977), is designed to rationalize the main anomalies in the travel-
distance framework and in cross-transporting. Starting with Miwani, that mill
is much closer than Chemelil to the areas farmed by the Kibisem, Nyang and
Harambee Cooperatives that their cane will be directed to Miwani, which indeed
received it until a few years ago. Likewise, immediately east of Chemelil
mill the Godabuoro Settlement, the western end of Tamu and the Twinbridge
sector of Songhor Settlement have been switched from Muhoroni to Chemelil.
The Muhoroni mill shortfall should be made up by accepting from points that
are equidistant, either from there or from Chemelil, all the production that
was previously routed sometimes to one of those mills, sometimes to the other;
the farms concerned are in the Ainomotua valley upstream of Kapurte Hill and
Senetwa. An equitable redistribution of cane supplies among the three mills is
expected to result from this rezoning. In order to assure a smooth transition,
Government, at negotiations, would furnish a plan of implementation of Legal
Notice No. 227 over a 3-4 year period, acceptable to the Bank/IDA. Expansion
of cane at the top of the Nandi Escarpment is not provided for under the
Project as the economic justification of the steep road link to Miwani is
unproven as yet; funds are included, however, for a study (Appendix 1).
9.        Climate for Sugarcane.  Tate and Lyle 1/ dealt thoroughly with
rainfall and temperature in relation to cane. Some climatic observations not
utilized by Tate and Lyle have been examined, in particular the full 1904/76
monthly rainfall log for Muhoroni and the 1923/76 data for Miwani. These
long records give the following monthly means and monthly drought risk factors:

ANNEX 2
Page 4
Except in July, Muhoroni has a slightly higher chance than Miwani each month
of receiving less than 50 mm of rain. The 50 mm rainfall is a threshold
below which cane would definitely suffer from stunting and temporary wilting.
However, the similarities between the two mill-sites are more important than
their divergencies. There appear to be only four months in the Nyanza Sugar
Belt when the likelihood is very high that rainfed cane will not be stressed
for water, namely March through May and August. Dry weather for cane ripening
and harvesting is virtually assured November-February at Miwani and October-
February at Muhoroni. January is the height of the dry season. Drought
hazard is probably higher still in the south-west and far west of the Sugar
Belt, where Ahero receives only 1,183 mm of rain annually and Kibos 1,280 mm,
than in the center. It is certainly lowest in the east, where for instance
Koru has a rainfall of 1,740 mm.

with that for Chemelil (1968/74). The monthly means are shown below; at all
these stations it is only in April and May that mean rainfall exceeds evapora-

Because most of the minor irrigation proposed under the Project is close
to Chemelil and Muhoroni, it suffices to use the less drastically severe
Chemelil evaporation data to calculate the irrigation need of sugarcane in
Annex 6. However, both on Ahero and in the proposed small irrigated cane
schemes at and near the National Sugar Research Institute, Kibos watering
rates should be based on the rather higher evapotranspiration expected at
that end of the Sugar Belt.

Page 5
11.       Temperature.  In the lower-lying and medium altitude portions of
the Nyanza Sugar Belt the mean temperature is close to 22 C without great
seasonal variations. Above Songhor and Koru, however, temperatures fall over
quite a short distance. The highest point in the Sugar Belt at which cane is
currently being grown may well be 1,950 m near Kamarero, above the River
Ainomotua. Several fields are around 1,800 m altitude where the mean
temperature throughout the year is likely to be about 18 C. This is decidedly
cool for cane, but every indication is that 24-month cuts (less for ratoons)
will warrant continuation of sugar-growing at such high elevations, provided
suitable soils 8n gentle to moderate slopes are used. The diurnal temperature
variation of 17 C results in a low level of plant respiration during the night
hours, conducive to a high sucrose content in the sugarcane.
12.       Geology.l/  Cane is grown mainly on deep alluvium and fairly deep
colluvium (2-4 m of unconsolidated soil mantle over hard rock) within the
Nyando and Kibos river basins, the upper catchments of which are floored
by igneous rocks, generally intermediate to acid, gneisses, granites and
pegmatites, as along the 600 m high south-facing Nandi Escarpment. In con-
trast, basic lavas predominate on the eastern hills, giving rise to soils

ANNEX 2
Page 6
much richer in plant nutrients than elsewhere (including nitrogen as the
high altitude coolness prevents rapid decomposition of organic matter).
A lower tableland of more resistant lava borders the south of the Sugar
Belt, between the Nyando and Kaplelach streams. Some of the gentle col-
luvial gradients and all the large stretches of alluvial flats, together
constituting the Kano plain, rest on beds of volcanic ash, lacustrine clay
and mudstone which are very slowly permeable. This formation extends westward
to the shores of Lake Victoria. Thin strips of alluvial levees and terraces
flank the main rivers throughout their courses and protrude above the water-
logged Kano plain deposits as sinuous "islands" of more pervious soils.
13.       Topography.  The altitude range in the Sugar Belt is about 1,130 m
from almost Lake Victoria level at Buoye (1,140 m) to a mountain summit in
Kunyak Settlement (2,270 m). However, nearly all cane fields lie between
1,150 m and 1,800 m on flat to gently sloping land at lower elevations, but
utilizing some fairly steep terrain, to about 20% gradient, along eastern
hillsides. Where the land slopes at less than about 1% and the soil, or
subsoil, is high in clay, a normal combination of circumstances, surface
drainage problems are encountered in cane fields (Annex 7). On the other
hand, gradients exceeding about 10% may be subject to erosion unless pre-
cautions are taken. No serious soil losses have as yet been incurred on large
areas of cane fields. At the foot of the Nandi Escarpment, from Kibos to
Chemase, there are sheet-eroded spots but many of these were probably caused
by presugar cultivation of annual crops, notably maize. Cane affords good
protection to rather steep land, compared with annuals, and it is universally
contour-planted in the Nyanza Sugar Belt. Provided this practice continues,
no special soil conservation measures will be required, other than cut of
drains to intercept down slope flows which would otherwise cause sheet and
gully erosion during heavy rains, but vigilance against any deterioration of
the satisfactory situation will be necessary.
14.       Altitude Bands for Sugarcane.  The physical environment differs
sufficiently throughout the Nyanza Sugar Belt to render worthwhile a
delineation of subdivisions of it with relatively uniform conditions of
geology, topography, and soils. The main determinant is elevation, so the
terms High (Altitude) Band, Medium Band and Low Band are used to distinguish
these regions (Maps IBRD 12988 and IBRD 12989). The High Band occupies the
hilly to mountainous areas above the generalized 1,400 m contour. The Low
Band fills the flood plains and adjacent mainly very gentle slopes of the
lower Nyando and lower Kibos alluvial basins; most cane fields in the Low Band
would benefit markedly from artificial drainage. The Medium Band has moderate
slopes and well drained or only slightly impermeable soils.
Maturation Period of Cane
15.       Although the recommended age at harvest is 22 months for plant
cane and 18 months for ratoons, indications are that in a normal season,
plant cane may be ready to harvest at 18-22 months and ratoons at 15-18
months. Due to factory limitations and particularly transportation problems,



- 12 -
ANNEX 2
Page 7
the estates and outgrowers have been producing more cane tonnage than can be
handled and harvesting of over-aged cane - frequently up to 30 month old plant
cane and 24 month old ratoons - has been the rule rather than the exception.
16.       Soil Classification.  The Kenya Soil Survey now uses the FAO/UNESCO
World Soil Map Legend, described by Dudal et al 1/ as the basis for its
identification of soil profiles and mapped soil units. In the Sugar Belt
there is a clear need for correlation between this new classification system
and those of the Songhor (1960) and Kano plain (1973) surveys. For two
kinds of soil that are important in cane production the equivalence appears
to be as follows:
Mbereri series (Songhor) = Conrad series (Kano)
- Rhodic Ferralsol (FAO/UNESCO). This is the main
well-drained red loam of the High and Medium Bands.
Kibegori series (Songhor) = Miwani series (Kano) = Pellic
Vertisol (FAO/UNESCO). This black cracking clay covers
the upper margins of the Low Band.
In the High Band as well as Ferralsols there are likely to be many represen-
tatives of Cambisols, including the most fertile dark brown loam to clay
profiles. In the Medium Band Acrisols and Ferralsols will be common, and
in the Low Band Gleysols in addition to Vertisols. The moderately vertic
(cracking and swelling) nature of potentially very productive land, with e.g.
Chemelil and Volo series, may well place them within Vertic Luvisols. Under
the Project, harmonizing the soil classification methods throughout the Sugar
Belt is proposed not merely to achieve pedological coordination but also as an
integral step in arriving at land evaluation procedures (Appendix 2).
17.       Land Evaluation.  Mapped suitability ratings specifically for
sugarcane would help in future decisions about expansion of cane fields,
reallocation from cane to another crop or vice versa, the magnitude of
applicability with respect to new husbandry techniques, irrigability,
further drainage possibilities, further zoning revisions and planning of
infrastructure and services. An attempt is made in Maps IBRD 12990 and IBRD
12991 to indicate the suitability of the Sugar Belt for high-input cane
growing. The suitability classes used, S1 to S4, reflect the approach and the
nomenclature of the FAO 2/ framework for land evaluation. The Maps would
require checking and refining during the Project soil investigations (Appendix
1). Refinements are expected to take several forms, e.g. separate suitability
1/   R. Dudal and co-authors (1974) "The Soil Map of the World at Scale
1:5,000,000 - Explanatory Notes." FAO/UNESCO, Paris.
2/   Food and Agriculture Organization (1976) "Framework for Land
Evaluation." Rome.

ANNEX 2
Page 8
ratings for the main recommended cane varieties (Co 421 and Co 617); resolution
of uncertainties about cane performance on rather stony soils in the light of
yield and performance data to be accumulated over the next few years from new
fields in the High Band; and perhaps a concentration of effort on the more
difficult and intractable land to drain, after experience has been gained with
the Project drainage scheme.
18.       Soil Analytical Data.  Tate and Lyle (1976) drew attention to the
main chemical factors limiting cane production. Fresh results both from
the National Agricultural Laboratory, Nairobi, and from the sugar companies,
confirm that N and P are the important deficient nutrients. The median content
of the topsoil nitrogen at 30 sites representing the lighter, more permeable
soils growing cane in the Sugar Belt is 0.16% and the heavier, less permeable
soils (70 samples) contain 0.18% total topsoil N. Available phosphorus, by
the Bray No. 2 extraction methods, has been measured on more samples and
shows distinctly higher values in alluvial terrace deposits than elsewhere.
Selected results are for the Tennant series (well drained alluvium, 15 samples),
38 ppm; other lighter soils, 29 ppm; alluvial clay complex (15 samples), 33
ppm; other heavier soils (80 samples) median, 14 ppm. Under the Project, only
one fairly high level of P dressing is recommended as a safety measure for all
soils (Annex 6). Although potassium is occasionally rather low, i.e. less
than 0.1 milliequivalent K per 100 g soil, it would suffice to give all cane
only occasional maintenance dosages or no K whatever. Toxicity of Na should
not be a problem on the vast majority of cane fields. Miwani reported that
their most sodic soils responded well to an application of as little as 5 t/ha
gypsum. It may be that a sulphur lack was being corrected rather than a
sodium excess. The gypsum might also have acted as a physical amendment,
since it appeared to inhibit surface capping. This had previously been
a serious problem on slightly sodic soils especially in years, unlike 1977,
with no prolonged periods of deeply penetrating heavy rains. Soils which
are saline or alkaline or both certainly exist in the Sugar Belt, but avoid-
ing them for future cane farming would not be difficult. The build-up and
spread of sodic conditions in irrigated areas would be guarded against under
the Project by provision of adequate drainage for irrigated fields (Annex 4).
19.       Remedies for Soil Problems.  In order of importance as regards
area of cane fields affected, the main soil defects may be summarized as:
(a) Infertility; solution: N and P application (Annex 6).
(b) Impermeability and allied conditions of slight salinity
and/or slight alkalinity locally; solution: while
improved drainage can be provided to remove surface
water accumulations, thereby permitting quicker drying
of field after heavy rain, it is questionable whether
the internal drainage of the less permeable soils can
be significantly improved at an economist cost. In these
high density closely packed soils the percentage of total
soil moisture which is available to the plant is low and



- 14 -
ANNEX 2
Page 9
even when some improved drainage can be achieved the benefits
may be largely effected by the earlier onset of drought conditions
in the dry season. The following would appear to offer the best
solutions to the problems of these soils:
(i) provision of adequate drains to intercept surface run-
off and prevent the collection of surface water in low-
lying areas.
(ii) reducing the harvesting season to not more than about
8 months in an average year to avoid excessive compaction
caused by transport equipment traversing the fields in the
wet season. Harvesting over 9 months might be considered
a maximum during years of particularly heavy cane yields.
(iii) providing more pronounced ridges along the cane rows by
using better earthing up practices. Growing of cane on
cambered beds will undoubtedly prevent ponding of surface
water providing the cane rows run across rather than along
the bed. Access for interrow cultivation and harvesting
would however be difficult and construction and main-
tenance of such a system would be costly. Its economic
justification would probably depend on the extent to which
soil internal drainage might be improved and it would be
worthwhile undertaking trials of the system.
(c) Lack of available moisture in the root zone, accompanied
by access to dry-season water supplies; solution: irriga-
tion (Annex 4).
(d) Stoniness; solution: assuming that stone removal is not
contemplated, avoidance of the land concerned or, if
trials prove successful, persistence with cane-growing
amid the stones. It is unclear as yet how stony a
possible field must be to warrant rejection. The mission
recommends that all stony soils derived from basic lavas
in the High Band, downgraded by Thorp et al (1960) to the
unfit class for cane, should be closely examined before
discarding, for already in the foothills of Tinderet
good cane is growing on such land, provided enough
interstitial soil is present.
20.       Focus on Best Cane Soils.  Miwani and Chemelil nucleus estates have
information on cane yields by soil series which is useful in forecasting
future cane performance. At Miwani, while most poorly permeable fields
with standard inputs (i.e. excluding drainage) have recently been yielding
about 26 tons of cane per ha yearly, the Medium Band well-drained soils of
medium texture, such as Mbereri series, have produced around 50 t/ha annually.
On the Ngeta section of Chemelil estate the Kundos series, the most nearly



ANNEX 2
Page 10
comparable soil to Mbereri, yields at about 53 t/ha annually, whereas the
black Kibegori clays produce, when drained at best 42 t/ha annually. Dark
brown clays in the Chemelil Low Band outyield all other soils in that Band.
Chemelil series produces up to 60 t/ha cane annuaLly and Volo series up to
58 t/ha both with high inputs including adequate artificial drainage. Al-
though no firm yield estimates for large areas are yet to hand, High Band
soils derived from basic lavas (e.g. Kipsesin, Martin and Sossok series)
will probably give on average 60-70 t/ha cane yearly under good management.
Wherever the logistics and the terrain permit development, such land should
be brought into sugar growing.
Ramisi Zone
21.       Location.  Ramisi estate occupies three parcels of land strung out
along the Mombasa-Lungalunga road for a distance of 45 km (Map IBRD 12997).
The factory is 70 km by road southwest of Mombasa at 4032'S latitude and
39024'E longitude, 2 km from the Indian Ocean coast. At present, the few
outgrowers have cane fields within 18 km of the mill. No factory zoning is
necessary, and cane supplies have in the past and can in future come from
anywhere within economic haulage radius, with the important proviso regarding
future production that only the relatively small proportion of good soils in
the semicircle of mill hinterland should be developed.
22.       History.  Before 1927, when Ramisi factory was built, the area
produced coconuts from a few plantations but was largely settled by people
of Digo stock. Their descendants farm the land surrounding Ramisi estate
and other large properties. Since its inception the sugar industry here
has employed as cane-cutters generally not the local people but migrant
laborers from the Nyanza Sugar Belt and environs.
23.       Demography.  From Map IBRD 12996 and tables of Swinson 1/ it can
be judged that the Ramisi sug r zone in the broadest sense had a 1969 popula-
tion of 42,000 on some 600 km . Swinson cites the growth rate as 3.4% annually.
On that basis, it is estimated that in 1979 the gone will have 61,000 inhabit-
ants. The 1969 population qensity was 70 per km , de5reasing from north
(Msambweni location, 105/km ) to south (Pongwe, 29/km ).
24.       Land Tenure and Type of Farming.  Apart from the sugar company
estate and a few other large farms, the Ramisi area is characterized by
small subsistence farms. Among the cash crops produced are cashews, mangoes,
cassava and limes. Maize and sweet potatoes are the principal food crops.
Cultivation is done by hand but bullock are common, with contract tractor
operators serving a few locations. Land adjudication has been completed
on most of the area.
1/  W. Swinson, "Existing and Projected Population in Planning Areas of
Kwale District." Special Rural Development Program, Kwale, Coast
Province, 1975.

25.       Communications.  There are no public railways or scheduled air
routes serving Ramisi. The nucleus estate has an airstrip. The Mombasa-
Tanga tarred road, the A-14, traverses the area. West and north of the
nucleus estate the murram road C-108 runs from Mrima via Kikoneni to Galu.
Several Class D and E roads, upon most of which little maintenance is
carried out, link the C-108 to the A-14.
26.       Climate for Sugarcane.  There are no temperature constraints to
cane growth at Ramisi, where the mean air temperature of 27 0C varies little
from month to month. However the diurnal temperature variation of only about
60 C results in a high plant respiration rate coinciding with the high night
time temperature which adversely affects the sucrose in cane. Rainfall and
drought risk data for Ramisi (1927/76) are presented below, along with open-
water Class A pan evaporation figures for Port Reitz near Mombasa (1950/66),
which are representative of Kenya's south coast.

For Mombasa there are 87 years of rainfall records (1890/1976) and the
monthly drought risk values over that whole period do not differ signif-
icantly from those over the last half-century. The Ramisi drought hazards
are, by analogy, therefore good indicators of the long-term situation, and
the varying shortages of water for rainfed cane from August through March
stand out clearly. Examination in detail of the Ramisi record reveals,
however, that averaging of the monthly rains from August to December does
not bring out the fact, of more benefits in cane planting than to standing
crops, that often one of those five months has heavy rains in an individual
year. Ramisi data reflect the climate of most of its zone, although the
Kikoneni area and Shimba foothills have a rather more reliable "little rains"
season in October-December.



ANNEX 2
Page 12
27.       Geology and Topography.  The Shimba Hills are grit and sandstone
uplands rising to 400 m some 25 km from the coast near Ramisi. Beneath them
there are dissected plateaux also resting on sandstone and, lower down, on
unconsolidated reddish sands, the Magarini formation, in the vicinity of
Kikoneni where the smallholder sugarcane currently farthest inland is being
planted, at elevations around 130 m. The Magarini beds are set as geomor-
phologically eroded residual tablelands amid a "sea" of the coarser greyish
Kilindini sands, which lap up to about the 40 m contour. Within a band along
the shore, usually 3-5 km wide, there are coral limestone raised beaches
characterized by pockets of soil in solution hollows among outcrops of jagged
reef.
28.       Soil Classification.  In the nomenclature of Michieka et al (1977)
the nucleus estate canelands have predominantly Albic Arenosols, grey to white
sands overlying Kilindini beds, as their growth medium. In shallow depres-
sions there are Gleyic Luvisoils, whose heavier profiles are rather slowly
permeable. Some small cane fields can be carved out of the coral rag,
wherever the pockets of Rhodic Ferralsols (red sandy loam over sandy clay)
coalesce sufficiently. Most cane outgrowers are farming land similar to that
at Ramisi. A significant minority of the existing sugar plantations are,
however, on Orthic and Rhodic Ferralsols formed from the Magarini strata, the
deep brown and red loams on Mrima section of the nucleus estate and at some
places east of that, to Kigomberu. Alone among the zone's soils, these have
in combination a fairly good moisture retention capacity and yet no risk of
waterlogging or perched water table problems. A1L the soils are deficient in
nitrogen and phosphorus, the medium total N contents of 50 tested topsoils
being 0.08% and median available P in 140 samples being 15 ppm. Potassium is
reasonably well supplied in many soils; the median topsoil value approaches
0.2 milliequivalents per 100 g soil for 140 samples. This low fertility status,
made worse by rapid leaching of nutrients during the rainy season limits the
growth of cane during a period when temperature and moisture availability are
optimal. The present practice of leaving a heavy mulch of trash from the un-
burnt cane in ratoon fields may further reduce available N in ratoons while
doing little to conserve moisture in dry weather. The rapid onset of drought
conditions in the dry season results in moisture availability being a con-
straint over much of the remainder of the year. The combined effects of these
constraints severely limit cane yields under rainfed conditions.
29.       Land Elevation.  The delineation on Map IBRD 12997 of the potential
for sugarcane culture gives an idea of the very varied resource base in Ramisi
zone. The objective of expansions to new rainfed cane areas would be to
maximize soil moisture conservation by choosing brown and red loam (Orthic and
Rhodic Ferralsol) tracts of land with gentle to moderate slopes - where
possible not exceeding about 12% gradient. On the map the patchy distribution
of these Class SI areas is apparent. The Ramisi estate plans fit well with
the overall aim, as the desirable soils and slopes cover most of the Mrima-
Bumbani extension and some of the Kuramojo valley new lands (the rest of that
block is fairly steep). Outgrower tracts would not, however, prove easy to
site judiciously, except around Kikoneni-Kigomberu and in a few other locali-
ties. Great caution would have to be exercised before going ahead with any



- 18 -
ANNEX 2
Page 13
expansion due north of the factory, along the Ramisi River, as sandy loose
Arensols dominate that valley. Likewise the Pongwe-Majoreni sector does not
seem to be a very good prospect for sugarcane; it has an alternation of
Arenosols with Gleyic Luvisols or even more swampy Gleysols. These are
physical determinants of that locality's low population density (Annex 4).
Limited expansion might be possible there, but under the Project there would
be a more certain outcome, in terms of expected yields (up to 50-53 tons per
ha yearly), if other areas within 25 km of the mill were developed, including
parts of Marenji Forest near the road, more land north of Mrima Hill, and a
good locality east of Mafisini. Yet further developments on red loams could
be envisaged if at any time an addition to the proposed 9,000 or 10,000 ha of
cane were to be contemplated. The clearing costs in savannah and open wood-
land which the Class SI soils carry would not be unduly high, especially if
advantage were taken of shifting cultivation fallow areas and heavily forested
groves are avoided. On most existing cane fields with poor excessively
permeable soils remedies for defects are difficult to invoke. Localized
impermeability could be overcome by better land shaping and improved husbandry.
There would be no need to undertake a separate scheme to ameliorate the
drainage. Over the small area for which irrigation water could be provided
under the Project, no highly retentive soils occur, so the benefits of
sprinkler irrigation (though superior to gravity feed of water) would not be
as great as they would in better cane-growing terrain.

Appendix 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Nandi Hills Extension Study
1.        In an effort to increase available cane supplies in the Miwani
zone, Government has been supporting a project to establish some 1,200 ha
of cane on the Nandi escarpment, about one-third of which has actually
been planted. The designated area on the escarpment is at an altitude of
about 1,800 m, or about 600 m above the Sugar Belt cane areas. Rainfall is
plentiful, but the high altitude and lower temperatures slow cane growth
somewhat; average yields are -expected to match the best yields in the
present Miwani area.
2.        The main constraint in the Nandi Hills development are the dis-
tance from the Miwani factory, which is 35 km from the remotest point, and
a steep climb of about 14 km on a low-standard gravel road which would make
cane transport costly and hazardous. Test shipments conducted to date have
not proven satisfactory. An upgrading of the road would be necessary, al-
though the degree and cost of upgrading cannot be determined without an
engineering study. A major improvement to tarmac standard, should such prove
necessary, could be very costly in that extremely difficult terrain, and the
limited possibilities for cane development in the Hills may make heavy
infrastructure investments economically unattractive. Although expansion
possibilities to a reasonable volume, excluding the escarpment, already
would be realized in the existing zone under the Project, the Miwani factory
could be adapted to absorb the additional cane from the escarpment.
3.        The Project would provide funds to determine the suitability of
the Nandi cane farming extension area for cane production and to make an
assessment of the cost of production of cane and of transport to the Miwani
factory. Suitable modes of transport would be identified and an engineering
study would be carried out as a basis for estimating road construction and
maintenance costs. The study would also cover alternatives of utilizing
the cane-growing potental on the Nandi Hills, such as establishing a small
sugar factory in the new area. A full financial and economic evaluation
would be made of the alternatives considered.
4.        The study would be conducted by the Kerya Sugar Authority with
the assistance of consultants; approximately 10 months of expert services
would be required. The cost is estimated at KShs. 736,000 (KShs 919,000
or US$110,000 including contingencies); further details are shown in
Annex 11, Table 33.
December 20, 1977

Appendix 2
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Summarized Specifications for Further Soil Investigations
in the Nyanza Sugar Belt
1.        The aims of the proposed studies would be:
(a) To harmonize the legends of existing soil maps and
to reclassify the soils, with remapping where needed,
in the current Kenya Soil Survey nomenclature.
(b) To evaluate the soils, in the field and by interpreting
laboratory findings, and the other relevant environmental
factors (essentially climate, topography, hydrology)
for rainfed sugarcane grown with appropriate management
and material inputs.
(c) To fill up gaps in the mapping of soils and of land
suitability for cane.
(d) To characterize the physical and chemical nature of the
soil on land to be (i) irrigated and (ii) drained.
2.        The area concerned covers about 100,000 ha including the 40,000 ha
of cane planned under the Project. Irrigability studies on at most 3,000 ha
and drainage soil mapping on at most 16,000 ha would be needed.
3.        The scale of the investigation would be semidetailed with 1:50,000
presentation (as the existing Songhor and Kano plain soil maps). Soil
inspections would be made every 25-30 ha on land not previously surveyed,
i.e. Miwani nucleus estate and neighboring large farms, the upper Ainomotua
valley and the eastern and southern fringes of Muhoroni zone. Two soil
scientists, either both Kenyan or an expatriate specialist and a counterpart,
would undertake the study.
4..       The timing should be as soon as new Nyanza Sugar Belt airphotos are
available (Annex 7), with no need to wait for contoured 1:5,000 engineering
plans. The probable duration of this element of the Project would be two
years. The report text and maps would be submitted to the Permanent Secretary,
Ministry of Agriculture. The main users of the findings would be agricultural
departments of the three factories and staff of the National Sugar Research
Institute.
5.        The cost would be KShs. 1.67 million (US$210,000) including pro-
vision for purchase and running of two 4-wheel drive vehicles.

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Project Sugar Zones
A. Introduction
1.        A sugar zone comprises a sugar factory, the factory's nucleus estate
and small and large outgrower farms. The four Project sugar companies owning
the factories and nucleus estates are very dissimilar with respect to owner-
ship, management, organization and structure of agricultural production, the
physical condition of factory assets, financial results etc., due partly to
historical factors and partly to environmental conditions. In this annex, the
companies' ownership, organization and management aspects are described first,
followed by factory and then financial aspects. The zones' outgrower situa-
tion is presented last. A detailed description of agricultural aspects is
included in Annex 6.
B. Ownership, Organization and Management of
Project Sugar Companies
Miwani
2.        Miwani Sugar Mills, Ltd., established in 1923, is a private company
owned by an Asian family with a primarily Asian management team. Employing
1,400 permanent staff with eight expatriates, Miwani has a highly centralized
management system. The company has a long history of good relations with the
large farmers in the zone who are primarily Asians. At present, Miwani does
not provide services or credit to outgrowers and the agricultural program is
concerned only with the nucleus estate. The company is managed by a Board of
Directors and a General Manager. There are four departments, the Accounting
Department, Factory Department, Personnel Department and the Technical Depart-
ment (Agriculture).
Chemelil
3.        The Chemelil Sugar Company, Ltd., formed in 1964 is 97% Government-
owned through the Agriculture Development Cooperation and the Development
Finance Company of Kenya (Annex 1). The company is managed by Bookers Agri-
cultural and Technical Services, Ltd., of the United Kingdom on a management
agreement with no equity interest. The agreement, which is effective for a
period of five years commencing March 16, 1976, is renewable and provides for



- 22 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2
a fixed annual management fee plus a commission on profits. The Bookers
management team has been reduced to eight expatriates from over 20 originally
who have been replaced by Kenyans after a prolonged period of training Kenyan
managers and technicians. The company is now in a transition period of
improving industrial relations and efficiency. Serious employee relation
problems which plagued the company in the past apparently have now been satis-
factorily resolved; the company has about 1,500 employees. After many years
of difficulty in dealing with small-scale outgrowers, Chemelil in recent years
has been working by contract with the Sugar Belt Cooperative Union and the
cooperative societies. This arrangement has improved relations and increased
production. Under the Board of Directors there is a General Manager and four
departments, i.e., the Accounting Department, Factory Department, Personnel
Department, and the Agriculture Department.
Muhoroni
4.        East African Sugar Industries, Ltd., founded in 1962, is owned
jointly by the Agriculture Development Corporation (84%) and the Mehta Group,
Ltd. (16%). The Mehta Group manages the company through its Uketa Development
Corporation, Ltd., a limited liability company incorporated in Kenya. The
management agreement has been concluded for a period of seven years, effective
December 5, 1973. The management fee consists of a fixed annual amount and
additional fees and a commission based on annual profits. Despite its major-
ity Government ownership the company has more of the characteristics of the
private companies because of the management system employed by the Mehta
Group. With a permanent staff of 550, there are 17 expatriate managers and
technicians. Up to the present, Muhoroni has operated as a factory with no
nucleus estate, and no agricultural department. The-privately-owned Nyando
Estate is being acquired for a nucleus estate and an agricultural department
is being formed; completion of the purchase would be a condition of negotia-
tions. Under the Board of Directors and General Manager there are four
departments, i.e. the Work Department (Factory), Personnel and Administration
Department, Accounting Department, and the Stores Department.
Ramisi
5.        The Associated Sugar Company, Ltd., established in 1922 and employ-
ing over 1,000 permanent staff, is a subsidiary of EMCO Kenya, Ltd., which
is privately owned by the Madhvani Group, a diversified international con-
glomerate which formerly operated sugar factories in Uganda. A new management
agreement between EMCO Kenya Ltd. and Ramisi was concluded in March, 1977.
The heavily centralized management system employs primarily expatriates in
senior positions. The company, under the direction of a General Manager, has
seven departments: Head Office, Accounting, Stores, Industrial Relations,
Medical, Factory and Plantation.



- 23 -
ANNEX 3
Page 3
D. Sugar Factory Aspects
6.        Chemelil and Muhoroni are relatively new factories commissioned in
the later 1960's. Miwani and Ramisi are older factories commissioned in the
1920's. The three factories in the Nyanza area have been modernized and
expanded in recent years and are in generally good condition. The Ramisi
factory has developed and expanded over the years primarily by installing
second-hand machinery and the condition of the factory is generally poor.
Cane and Sugar Production
7.        All of the Project sugar factories are c:haracterized by inadequate
cane supplies. The combined present rated annual production capacity of the
four factories is about 7,300 tons of cane per day (TCD), or 190,000 tons of
sugar as shown in Table 1. The actual production in 1976 was only 103,600
tons or about 54% of available production capacity. In the Nyanza Sugar Belt,
total annual cane and sugar production has not varied significantly since
about 1969 (as shown in Table 2).  The annual differences are within limits
for rainfed cane cultivation practices in the area.
Factory Equipment and Capacity
8.        Chemelil and Muhoroni have recently undertaken rehabilitation and
expansion programs of their relatively new factories. Equipment is generally
in good condition and ample capacity is installed at most production stations
to handle present production rates. The planned expansion capacity for
Chemelil was 2,880 TCD and for Muhoroni, 2,600 TCI). Present capacities for
both factories are limited by production bottlenecks in the milling station.
In the case of Chemelil, the expansion program was halted before completion
because sufficient cane supplies could not be developed to utilize the planned
expansion capacity. Equipment installation is still not completed for the
Muhoroni expansion program because of delays in equipment deliveries and prob-
lems in comissioning the new equipment. The present capacity is 2,300 TCD
for the Chemelil factory and 2,000 TCD for the Muhoroni factory.
9.        The Miwani factory has been modernized and expanded over the years.
New equipment was installed and equipment was maintained for many production
stations resulting in duplication and a congested factory layout. Present
factory capacity is 2,000 TCD. Some rehabilitation and replacement of equip-
ment would be necessary to insure the operational reliability and long-term
viability of the factory at its present capacity.
10.       The Ramisi factory is in poor condition.  Factory capacity was
increased haphazardly over thie years. A DDS Type II bagasse diffusor has
been installed for years but has never been operated because of insufficient
factory power. A second-hand boiter and new turbogenerator are presently
being instaaled to provide additional steam and electric power.  Present
factory capacity is 1,000 TCD but the operating rate is only about 600 to 700
TCD. Considerable rehabilitation and replacement of equipment would be
necessary to niaintain the loag-term viability of the factory at its present
capacity.



- 24 -
ANNEX 3
Page 4
Manufacturing Process
11.       All of the factories use three-roller mills to extract juice from
cane. Juice treatment and sugar boiling practices vary among the factories
which all produce a poor quality white sugar. All product sugars are packed
in 100 kg net weight bags. Miwani and Ramisi both use the double sulphitation
process. Chemelil uses the juice sulphitation process. Muhoroni uses the
lime defecation-melt process to produce white sugar. Process control is gen-
erally poor and inadequate. The sugars produced by the factories contain a
high amount of insoluble matter for direct consumption sugar and do not meet
Kenya Sugar Standards for color and, occasionally, for polarization.
Factory Performance
12.       Sugar recoveries are low in the Project factories.  The overall
recovery of sugar from cane depends on the quality of cane and the quality of
work performed in the factory. The quality of factory work is dependent on
the available equipment and staff. At current low production rates, the
factories have excess equipment capacity so that equipment limitation are
usually not a constraint. Limitations of the management staff and operating
personnel are probably the primary reason for the poor factory performance.
Optimizing factory sugar recovery requires continuous attention to the details
of production operations including the organization and coordination of cane
deliveries.
13.       Cane quality is generally uniform across the Nyanza Sugar Belt so
that incoming materials to the three Project factories are generally equiv-
alent. In 1976, overall recoveries ranged from 71% at Miwani to 79% at
Chemelil as shown in Table 3. Muhoroni's overall recovery was 75%. The
current overall sugar recovery levels are substantially below standards
acceptable in sugar industries elsewhere. At present cane quality levels,
overall recoveries in the range of 83% would be a reasonable target for the
short term. Long-term improvement to levels above 85% are feasible. In 1976
the Mumias factory achieved an overall recovery of 85%. Ramisi's overall
recovery was only 71%.
By-Products
14.       Molasses is presently the only significant by-product of the sugar
factories. Bagasse is utilized as fuel to generate steam for the factory
power system and filter cake is plowed into the fields as a fertilizer and
soil conditioner. Molasses is exported or used locally to produce alcohol
and cattle feed. Over the past three years, molasses exports averaged about
33,000 tons, or 53% of the average annual production of about 65,000 tons.
15.       Miwani operates a distillery capable of producing about 1.5 million
litres of 960 v/v ethyl alcohol per year. The distillery capacity is adequate
to supply all of Kenya's requirements for industrial alcohol. In the future



ANNEX 3
Page 5
the distillery will also produce a limited amount of potable alcohol under a
contract with Gilbey's. About 50% of Miwani's current molasses production
is used for alcohol production.
16.       Chemelil produces a molasses and urea mix for use as cattle feed;
about 5,000 tons, or 30% of the company's current molasses production is used
for this purpose. Local molasses sales are controlled and limited to holders
of permits issued by the District Commissioner.
D. Financial Aspects of Project Sugar Companies
17.       Two important factors are paramount in determining the sugar
companies' financial performance:
(a) the Sugar Belt factories have been operating at 50-60%
of available production capacity; and
(b) Chemelil, Muhoroni and Ramisi have not had enough capital.
Miwani
18.       The revenues of Miwani were KShs. 58.7 million in 1976 which rep-
resents a 42% increase from 1973. The variable production costs increased
from KShs. 620/t in 1973 to KShs. 1,428/t in 1976, and the total cost of
sugar per ton increased from KShs. 895 in 1973 to KShs. 2,182 in 1976. The
net income of Miwani was KShs. 1.4 million in 1976 which represents 2% of
revenues. The company's net income has fluctuated since 1973 within the range
of 1% to 2% of revenue as well as equity. In 1976 the company's equity re-
serves were KShs. 96.7 million, retained earnings KShs. 3.2 million, current
assets KShs. 13.1 million and current liabilities KShs. 20.2 million; thus the
current ratio was 0.6. There were no long-term debts in 1976.
Chemelil
19.       The revenues of Chemelil were KShs. 104.9 million in 1976, or 168%
above 1973 levels. The variable production costs increased from KShs. 753/t
in 1973 to KShs. 1,151/t in 1976. Over the same period, the total cost of
sugar per ton increased from KShs. 1,280 to KShs. 1,883. The 1976 net income
of Chemelil was KShs. 22.4 million and represented 21% of revenues. The com-
pany's net income since 1973 has fluctuated within the range of -65% to 21%
of revenue and -49% to 13% of equity. The company's equity was increased from
KShs. 52.2 million in 1973 to KShs. 173.2 million in 1976. Cumulative losses
were KShs. 114.0 million in 1973 and KShs. 115.0 million in 1976. The current
ratio in 1976 was 2.9, while the long-term debt to equity ratio was 25:75
excluding retained earnings, and 49:51 including retained earnings.



- 26 -
ANNEX 3
Page 6
Muhoroni
20.       The revenues of Muhoroni were KShs. 62.2 million in 1976 which rep-
resents a 66% increase from 1973. The variable production costs increased
from KShs. 634/t in 1973 to KShs. 1,299/t in 1976, and the total cost of
sugar per ton increased from KShs. 915 to KShs. 1,982.   The company's net
income was KShs. 7.6 million in 1976, representing 12% of revenues. Its net
income since 1973 has fluctuated within the range of 7% to 12% of revenue,
and 9% to 28% of equity. In 1976 equity reserves amounted to KShs. 27.6
million and retained earnings to KShs. 8.4 million. The current ratio in
1976 was 1.4; the long-term debt to equity ratio was 57:43 excluding retained
earnings, and 51:49 including retained earnings.
Ramisi
21.       The revenues of Ramisi were KShs. 14.5 million in 1976, or only
0.7% above those of 1973. The variable production costs increased from
KShs. 872/t in 1973 to KShs. 1,802/t in 1976. The total cost of sugar
per ton increased from KShs. 1,250 in 1973 to KShs. 2,845 in 1976. The
company in 1976 had a net loss of KShs. 2.7 million which represents 19%
of the revenues. Net income since 1973 fluctuated within the range of
-11% to -19% of revenue, and -16% to -28% of equity.  In 1976 the company s
equity reserves were KShs. 9.8 million, cumulative losses KShs. 11.8 million,
and net worth minus KShs. 2.0 million. The current ratio was 1.2, and the
long-term debt to equity ratio, excluding retained earnings, was 69:31 in
1976. The company has a parent company loan of KShs. 17.6 million, interest-
free, in lieu of sufficient equity capital. From a financial point of view,
Ramisi would be hard pressed to become a viable company without the type of
remedies proposed under the Project.
E. Sugarcane Outgrowers
22.       All sugar companies process sugarcane produced by outgrower farmers
in addition to cane produced on their own estates or, in the case of Muhoroni,
the Nyando Estate. Outgrowers' farms vary in size from a few hectares to more
than 2,000 ha. Organizationally, some outgrowers are independent, or unaffil-
iated with any of the farmers' representative bodies, but the majority are
members either of the Sugar Settlement Scheme or a cooperative society.
The Sugar Settlement Scheme
23.       Established in 1964, the 24,300 ha Sugar Settlement Scheme, located
in the Muhoroni zone of the Sugar Belt is governed by the Settlement Fund
Trustees (S.F.T.) acting through the Settlement Department of the Ministry of
Lands and Settlement. Policies are established by the Board of Directors of
the Sugar Settlement Organization which is comprised of representatives
of the Ministry of Agriculture, Cooperatives, Lands and Settlement, the Kenya
Sugar Authority, and one representative each from the seven cooperative
socieites in the scheme. Management responsibility rests with the Executive



- 27 -
ANNEX 3
Page 7
Controller, appointed by the Trustees. The scheme is comprised of seven
units with a total of some 3,000 settlers. Each unit has a primary coopera-
tive society which jointly form the Muhoroni Cooperative Union. The union has
had serious management and financial difficulties and is now in Government
receivership.
24.       Within the Settlement Scheme, approximately 21,000 ha is agricul-
tural land of which 5,700 ha are planted to cane. Most of the plots are 4 ha,
with I ha designated for subsistence crops and home site and 3 ha for cane
production. The cane plots are grouped into large blocks of 50-100 ha or
more, ostensibly to facilitate mechanization and efficient management.
Actual production of cane in the blocks has been carried out by a variety
of different arrangements involving private contractors, the societies and
union and the S.F.T. Originally, the S.F.T. performed all agricultural,
harvesting and transport operations. Gradually, some operations, particularly
those requiring hand labor, were turned over to the societies and the indivi-
dual farmers. With the collapse of the union, these functions have reverted
back to S.F.T. which still provides the credit, inputs and most farming
services. The costs of the cane farming operation have exceeded the charges
to the farmers and the S.F.T. has absorbed these excess costs from Ministry
funds, in effect providing a subsidy to the settlement farmers.
25.       A serious problem in the Settlement Scheme is the existence of
settlers who, for a variety of reasons, are not permanent, resident, full-
time farmers. Many of the settlers were not farmers when they were settled.
Part of this group was comprised of urban residents who were either unemployed
or marginally employed. The Government's purpose was to solve the growing
problems in the cities by resettling people in one of the settlement schemes.
These settlers tend to be part-time farmers, depending on jobs in Kisumu,
Nairobi or elsewhere for their main income and sometimes make arrangements
with a resident farmer to "manage" their property or have a second family or
other relative on the property. Some non-resident settlers in the Settlement
Scheme are civil servants living in the cities who are even less inclined to
become full-time farmers. Most of the part-time absentee farmers are parti-
cipating in block-farming schemes managed by S.F.T. So far, block-farming
arrangements have not paid off well for most of the land-holders. Conse-
quently, their incentive to become full-time resident farmers has been fur-
ther diminished. The repayment record on the land purchase and development
loans extended to settlers has been poor, with absentee farmers lagging behind
resident farmers. Of the total loans of KShs. 20 million extended, about
KShs. 12 million is currently in arrears.
26.       While no figures are readily available, it is estimated that the
percentage of civil servants in the scheme is small, perhaps 10%. But all
absentee and part-time farmers together could constitute as much as 50% of the
total settlers, the balance being full-time farmers who were settled from
other rural areas. The Ministry has the legal authority to evict settlers who
do not make payments or do not live on their land and farm it. Although the
repayment record is very poor and absenteeism is widespread, very few if any



- 28 -
ANNEX 3
Page 8
violators actually have been evicted. It appears that it would be virtually
impossible to seriously enforce the settlement laws because of political
repercussions.
27.       In summary, the basic problems of the Settlement Scheme are:
(i) the high percentage of absentee or part-time farmers; (ii) the large debt
burden of the farmers for land purchase and development costs which is accruing
compound interest at the rate of 6-1/2% on the arrears; (iii) the low level of
yields and profitability arising from poor soils, drainage problems, poor
agronomic practices, and bad management; (iv) the low level of participation
of the farmers in decision-making and actual farm operations, both being left
to the Settlement Organization; (v) the collapse of the Muhoroni Cooperative
Union, leaving the organization of farmers in disarray.
The Cooperative Movement
28.       The major elements of the cooperative movement in the Sugar Belt,
besides the Muhoroni Cooperative Union with its seven constituent societies
(para. 23) are the 24 marketing societies and 11 production societies affil-
iated with the Sugar Belt Cooperative Union (SBCU), and the unaffiliated
Nyanza Farmers Cooperative Society.
29.       The Marketing Societies.  These societies have been organized in
all three zones of the Sugar Belt since the early 1960s up to the present.
Some societies were formed in traditional villages of subsistence farmers
where there was land suitable for cane. Some large expatriate farms were
purchased by groups of Africans organized into "farm purchase societies"
which later became marketing societies. Most of the farmers in these soci-
eties operate under independent management. They contract the factory or a
private contractor for land preparation, with SBCU generally coordinating the
harvesting and transport. Production credit requirements are handled through
the Cooperative Production Credit Scheme (Annex 1) or Ministry of Agriculture
loans. Although functioning better than the Settlement Scheme or the col-
lective block system in the production societies, the marketing societies
are not producing what they could be. Lack of coordination, poor extension,
shortage of equipment for cultivation and transport, flooding, bad roads,
labor problems and farmer dissatisfaction all exist here as well.
30.       The Production Societies.  Production societies total about 1,800
farm families and hold about 3,800 ha of land of which 2,800 ha is currently
planted to cane. Many of these societies' members were settled by Government
on their land in the mid-1960's, primarily in the Chemelil zone, the others
were farmers who lived in the area previously. They were moved from their
traditional villages along the edge of Lake Victoria where the level of
the lake was raised and their villages were flooded. Each of the resettled
farmers received originally about 3.2 ha for cane and a 0.8 ha homestead for
subsistence crops and homesite. The cane plots were formed into large blocks
which were cultivated by the Chemelil sugar company. Operating primarily
through foreign contract firms and with Government and foreign assistance
loans, Chemelil attempted to produce cane with a centrally controlled program



- 29 -
ANNEX .
Page 9
which was essentially a collective system with no management decisions being
made by farmers. The field extension work with farmers was minimal. Farmers
could not identify with their individual plots of land and workec mainly as
paid casual hire laborers. While the cooperative societies were organized
from the outset, they received only minimal management assistance or training.
Agronomic practices were poor, yields generally low, late harvesting and
transport problems were common and net income to farmers was low. Farmers'
interest in the collective blocks declined and they began to produce "private"
cane under their own management on their subsistence plots.
31.       The Sugar Belt Cooperative Union (SBCU).  In an attempt to
strengthen the cooperative movemert and increase small-scale cane produc-
tion, the Government took the initiative in 1971 to organize the Sugar Belt
Cooperative Union, originally with 13 member societies. A private social
development foundation from Germany, the Friedrich-Ebert-Stiftung (FES),
has been providing financial assistance and advisors to SBCU since 1973.
Beginning in 1973, an agricultural department was established to identify
land for new cane planting, plan and supervise cultivation, and coordinate
harvesting and transport of cane. Its extension program has employed from
25-30 extension staff from the Ministry of Agriculture.
32.       The SBCU took over management control of agricultural operations
and credit and became the intermediary between farmers and the factory,
primarily in the Chemelil zone but also in Miwani and Muhoroni. The factory
became a tractor work contractor along with other private contractors.
However, the technical and management capability of SBCU has not been a
significant improvement over previous arrangements and the level of agronomic
practices and hence yields did not increase significantly. The SBCU has
maintained the decision-making responsibility and farm participation has not
increased sufficiently.
33.       Since 1973, SBCU has undertaken a rather ambitious program of
developing new cane land under strong initiatives from the Government. The
achievements have been generally mixed and in some respects, such as cane
maintenance, rather poor. Several loans were extended to SBCU, totalling
approximately KShs. 26.7 million to develop or rehabilitate some 6,640 ha of
cane. Cost overruns have been considerable and farmers' accounts have not
been kept up to date, creating serious financial difficulties for SBCU. Of
the KShs. 18 million still owed to the Government by SBCU about KShs. 13
million represent liabilities of individual farmers, not all of which are
either documented or recoverable. The Cooperative Production Credit Scheme,
established in 1974 to provide supervised production credit to individual
members growing private cane, promises to be more successful; repayments have
been good so far.
Medium and Large Farms
34.       The majority of these farmers are not African and have been farming
in the area for several generations; a transfer into African ownership has
been occuring at a slow but steady pace. Most of the large farmers are members



- 30 -
ANNEX 3
Page 10
of the Nyanza Farmers Cooperative Society, a non-affiliated society without
production or marketing functions which represents the members in negotiations
and discussions with the factories and Government.
Independent Farmers
35.       There are a number of farmers in SBCU and the Settlement Scheme
who grow cane on subsistence plots or other land without institutional credit,
agricultural or marketing services. They sell directly to the factories. A
significant number of other small and large farmers also are unaffiliated,
deal directly with the factory and are able to finance their operations
without organizational assistance.
Ramisi Outgrowers
36.       There are at present only 35' small outgrowers producing cane on
small plots of up to 4 ha at Ramisi, and a few large cane farmers. A coop-
erative society exists which mainly operates a cane transport lorry. There
is no cooperative credit. About 80 outgrowers currently have production
loans from AFC. The Kigombero Special Rural Development Scheme provides
some extension help and formerly provided credit but stopped because
repayment was poor.
December 9, 1977
