KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Irrigation
A. Introduction
1.        The rainfall pattern and its unreliability are one of the major
factors causing low cane yields both in the Nyanza Sugar Belt and at Ramisi.
Pilot schemes in the Sugar Belt demonstrate that yield increases of the order
30 t/ha per year could be obtained with irrigation.
2.        In the Sugar Belt the use of Lake Victoria as a water resource is,
because of cost, centered on schemes projected for implementation along the
immediate lake shore. The rivers in the Kano plains have a low dry season
flow. A proposal for the diversion of the Sondu River into the Kano plains
for power production and irrigation has not been followed up. At Ramisi
the rivers are not perennial and the problem also becomes one of storage.
Groundater could be an interesting source of supply but not enough work has
been done to determine the extent and firm yield of the acquifer or the
location of the saline interface. In these circumstances only limited,
minor irrigation can be envisaged at present, both for the Sugar Belt and
Ramisi.
B. Present Situation
Nyanza Sugar Belt
3.        The water available for irrigation in the Sugar Belt comes from
the Nyando River and its tributaries, and the Kibos river. A network of
gauging stations has been set up in the Nyando catchment of which 10 are
in the immediate vicinity of the Sugar Belt. There are two stations on the
Kibos. The data is being processed at present by the Hydrological Division of
the Ministry of Water Development but the results are not yet available.
However, the lowest flows ever recorded were extracted from the records and
used to determine the minimum water available for irrigation.
4.        The Nyaldo and its main tributary the Ainomotua have catchment
areas of 1,503 km and 1,373 km , respectively, at their confluence. The
minimui flow recorded at station 1GD3, downstream of th  confluence, was
2.00 m /sec in March 1976. Downstream demand is 1.00 m /sec (para. 7). In
the Nile Basin Water Resources Survey carried out by Sir Alexandsr Gibb and
partners in 1954-1956 the lowest base flow was estimated at 1.8 m /sec at
Ahero. The minimum flow of the Kibos recorded at station 1 HA 4 downstream



- 35 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2
of the Kisumu water supply intake was 0.27 m /sec in Mirch 1971 (Table 1).
This compares with the 20% probable exceedence (0.23 m /sec) given in the Gibb
report. The monthly discharges of the Nyando and the Kibos for various pro-
babilities need to be verified when analysis of the flow data, now being
undertaken, is complete. Only then can a firm decision be taken on water
availability and, hence, on the irrigable area.
5.        Crop water requirements are related to effective rainfall and Pan A
evaporation since the water demand of cane is approximately the same as Pan A
Evaporation (1,938 mm annual average). As cane needs to be dried off for two
months before harvesting and water demand is reduced for three months after
cutting, the theoretical estimated crop water requirement has been reduced by
24%. With an overall irrigation system efficiency Sf approximately 56%, the
headgate water requirements would be about 11,500 m /ha annually. Consider-
ing the minimum flow in the critical month (March), about 400 ha could be
irrigated from the Kibos river and about 2,000 ha from the Nyando and
Ainomotua rivers, irrigating 12 hours at peak (Table 1). This would not
compromise present irrigation at Ahero (para. 7). Analyses of water sampled
at Ahero pilot scheme show that the water quality is suitable for irrigation.
Irrigation water requirements are shown in Table 2.
6.        Soils in the Project area are described in detail in Annex 2.  There
are about 8,000 ha of soils suitable for furrow irrigation of cane in the
three Sugar Belt zones. This greatly exceeds the 2,000 ha for which water is
available for irrigation.
7.        There are existing pilot schemes under irrigation in the Kano plains
at Ahero and West Kano. These schemes are managed under Dutch technical
assistance provided by ILACO who have a six-year contract, of which four years
are still to run; FAO provided assistance for the period 1968-73. Ahero is
a pumped scheme utilizing run-of-the-river flow from the Nyando to irrigate
770 ha (gross) of rice and 30 ha (gross) of experimental sugarcane. Yield in-
creases under irriiation are discussed in Annex 6. Its present peak water
requirement of 1 m /sec 1/ occurs in May when the rice paddys are being
flooded. The West Kano scheme is planned for 400 ha of cane and 400 ha of
rice under irrigation using water pumped from Lake Victoria. At present,
about 200 ha of cane and 200 ha of rice are under cultivation. In both
cases the sugarcane is furrow irrigated. Furrow length is limited to 200 m
at a grade of 0.2%. At present 120 mm of water is applied through 2 1/sec
siphons every 18 days when about two-thirds of available water is used up.
Overall irrigation efficiency is claimed to be 60%.
1/   ILACO sources indicate peak requirements of up to 1.2 m /sec with
present irrigation efficiency levels.



- 36 -
ANNEX 4
Page 3
8.        Approximately 800 ha of cane was successfully irrigated by the
Miwani factory about a decade ago by pumping from the Nyando close to
Chemelil. After rezoning the land was allocated to Chemelil which dis-
continued the irrigation and allowed the system to fall into disrepair.
Ramisi Zone
9.        Mean annual rainfall for the area is about 1,200 mm of which about
700 mm falls from March to June (Annex 2). The water available for irriga-
tion comes from ae existing dam at Musembeni. The catchment area of this
dam is only 18 km which provide an annual runoff of about 1.5 Mcm with 90%
probability of exceedence, or about 5 Mcm with 50% probability. 1/ The dam
is stated to have a capacity of 2.7 Mcm at full supply level.  It con-
sequently does not fill something like one year in four. There is an exist-
ing, but unused, dam further upstream on the same river which the Associated
Sugar Company would like to rehabilitate to provide 0.9 Mcm of additional
storage. An alternative source of water is the Kuramojo river where2a
potential damsite has been identified. The catchment area is 130 km which
would yield approximately 7 Mcm with 90% probability. Boreholes in the area
(Appendix 1) have given yields from 5 to 45 m /ha but the quality of the water
is variable and, as has been stated, the extent of the acquifer is unknown.
10.       Crop water requirements calculated on the basis described in
para. 5 would be about 12,000 m /ha annually at the headgate using an overall
irrigation system efficiency of 76% 2/ (Table 2).   Seven surface water
samples taken in the Ramisi area were analyzed by the National Agricultural
Laboratories, Nairobi, and the water quality was found suitable for irrigation
in all cases.
11.       Soils in the Project area are suitable for irrigation but their
permeability (and the broken topography) makes sprinkler irrigation necessary.
At present, about 480 ha of cane is grown under sprinkler irrigation. Water
from the dam described in para. 9 is pumped in a 7 km concrete lined canal
leading to a booster pump station. The main pumps and motors are worn out
and need replacing and the canal lining is in poor condition and needs con-
siderable repair if excessive water losses are to be avoided.
12.       The booster pumps irrigate 373 ha through buried asbestos mains
fitted with hydrants every 122 m. Portable sprinkler lines, 366 m long, are
1/   Specific runoff:                   2

coupled to the hydrants and the spriliklers are moved along the portable lines.
Sprinklers have an application rate of 19 mm/hour, are moved every three
hours, and operate for 18 hours a day. Portable lines are moved eaily and
the irrigation cycle is 12 days. Ten applications are given during a
120-day period, i.e. 5,700 m /ha are applied during the irrigation period.
In addition, Ramisi has two sets of Wright Rain portable equipment irri-
gating 106 ha pumping directly out of the main canal. The application
rate is 13 mm/hour. Sprinklers are moved every 4-1/2 hours and operate
for 18 hours a day. The 5ycle is 12 days and 10 applications are given
in 120 days, i.e. 5,850 m /ha irrigation application. The cane in both
cases is receiving less than half its theoretical requirements.
13.       The total amount of water applied to the 480 ha irrigated is
2.75 Mcm, i.e. exactly the existing dam's capacity. The full water needs
of the irrigated cane cannot be met because, although the sprinkler system
installed has the capacity to handle 480 ha of irrigation, the volume of
water which can be stored is inadequate and the catchment area of the dam
is too small for it to fill every year.
C. The Project
Nyanza Sugar Belt
14.       The Project would develop run-of-river pumped irrigation schemes
on the Nyando, the Ainomotua and the Kibos rivers and would modify the exist-
ing irrigation system at Ahero to transform it from rice to cane production.
The new schemes, subject to confirmation of sufficient water supply (para. 4),
would be the following:
(a) 400 ha in the Miwani zone irrigated from the Kibos river.
The scheme would be located to include 80 ha of the Kibos
research station and the large farms immediately adjacent.
The integration of part of the Kibos station into the irri-
gation scheme is discussed in Annex 6.
(b) 920 ha of the Chemelil nucleus estate irrigated from the
Ainomotua. This would include most of the area previously
irrigated.
(c)  670 ha of the Nyando Estate at Muhoroni irrigated from
the Nyando.
Ramisi Zone
15.       The Project could not accept the Associated Sugar Company's pro-
posal to rehabilitate the existing small dam (para. 9). It is likely that
it would only fill in average or wetter than average years and there is no



- 38 -
ANNEX 4
Page 5
guarantee that it would improve the present water deficiency. The Project
would reconstruct the main pump station on the Musembeni dam and replace
the existing worn-out pumps and motors, provide for carrying out the repairs
necessary on the main canal lining to prevent excessive water losses so that
370 ha of the presently irrigated area can be irrigated adequately, and
construct a new dam on the Kuramojo river and provide irrigation equipment
for an additional 400 ha.
Project Components
16.       Land Preparation would involve land smoothing to a uniform gradient
before forming beds and furrows. Furrows would be set out to a gradient of
0.2% and would not exceed 250 m in length. 3It is estimated that this work
would in volve moving between 150 and 200 m of earth per ha.
17.       Main canals would be trapezoidal in shape with 1:1 side slopes,
and concrete-lined as required; construction would be in compacted bank where
necessary to give maximum command.
Miwani    Chemelil    Muhoroni
Main Canal Length (km)                 7.0         5.0         9.0
The canal capacities would be designed on the basis of an irrigation module
of 1.34 1/sec/ha and the canal section would vary from head to tail, reducing
at each turnout to a distributary. The gradient would be 0.05% giving
maximum velocities of 0.7 m/sec. The 7.5 cm concrete lining, necessary to
prevent excessive losses of pumped water, would be mesh reinforced.
18.       Distributaries would branch off the main canal every 250 m.  They
would be excavated in compacted bank to give the necessary command and would
be trapezoidal, with 1:1 side slopes and unlined. The top width of the
canal would be 1.30 m, and the depth 0.50 m.  Canals would be aligned normal
to the natural slope of the ground and set out to a gradient of 0.1%. Where
the ground slope exceeded this gradient the check structures feeding field
ditches would act as drops. Each distributary would command 40 ha and would
discharge a constant leading stream of 55 1/sec, irrigating only during the
day.
Miwani    Chemelil    Muhoroni       Total
Distributary Length (km)      16.0       37.2          26.8        80.0
19.       Field head ditches, 250 m long at 200 m intervals, would dis-
charge the leading stream from the distributaries to the 5 ha fields.
Each field would be irrigated in turn on an 8-day cycle, i.e. each
field ditch would be in use once in eight days. The canals would be in
compacted bank, 0.50 m above field level and aligned to a gradient of
0.1%. Dimensions would be as for distributaries.

21.       2" Diameter plastic siphons discharging 2 1/sec each would be

22.       The 400 ha irrigable area at Miwani would require drainage after
the installation of the irrigation network. The area falls outside the
the Sugar Belt drainage scheme and has not been included. The Project
would provide this area with a drainage layout similar to that described
in Annex 7. The irrigable areas at Chemelil and Muhoroni fall into the
drainage scheme.
23.       Pump stations for the three new schemes in the Nyanza Sugar Belt
would be equipped with electrically driven centrifugal pumps. Power supply is
available close to the proposed pumping sites at Chemelil and Muhoroni, and a
5 km power line would be constructed to the Kibos river pump site. Each
pumphouse would be equipped with three pumps operating under an estimated
total head of 30 m.

A silt trap would be added to the Ahero pump station.  At Ramisi the
pumps and motors in the main pump station require replacement and a new
pump station would be constructed, equipped with three 160 1/sec turbine
pumps driven by 50 hp diesel motors. The pumps and motors in the booster
pump station are still serviceable and do not need replacement. A new pump
station with two pump sets also would be provided for the Kuramojo dam.
24.       A small earthen dam would be constructed between Kuramojo and
Kivumuro on the Kuramojo river. The dam, some 150 m in length and 16.5 m in
height at spillway level, would have a slope of 1:3 upstream and of 1:2V5
downstream and require an approximate volume of earth work of 100,000 m .
Surveys, design and supervision of construction would be provided. Portable

25.       Ahero conversion to cane would require demolition of existing
paddy bunds in addition to the land forming described in para. 16.
Further inlet gates and check structures would be required to give the
necessary water control for furrow irrigation. Plastic siphons would
also be provided for field water application.

E. Organization and Management
28.       There has been no survey or detailed design of new irrigation works
in the Project areas. It is therefore proposed that a technical committee
should be formed within KSA comprising KSA staff, representatives from the
Ministry of Water Development and the National Irrigation Board (NIB) and the
irrigation and drainage engineer to be employed by the National Sugar Research
Institute, Kibos.  The committee would assist KSA in drawing up terms of
reference and in the selection and appointment of consultants for executing
the irrigation works. The consultants would examine the hydrology of the
basins to determine the area irrigable from each river. They would then carry
out all surveys and detailed design works necessary for drawing up contract
documents and calling for tenders. They would also provide site supervision of
the works to be constructed under contract. There are sufficient experienced
local contractors to carry out these works but international competitive
bidding procedures should be used.



- 42 -
ANNEX 4
Page 9
Cost Recovery
29.       The cost of the works would be borne by the sugar companies which
would directly benefit from the irrigation installation. At Chemelil,
Muhoroni and Ramisi the works are located on the Companies' nucleus estates
and would be owned by them. At Miwani the pump station and main canal
would be owned by the National Irrigation Board which would need to acquire
wayleaves from the private owners on whose land they would be situated.
Allowance for this has been made in the costs. The irrigation works would be
located on the Kibos Research Station and on eight or nine adjacent large
farms and would be owned by them. In this case NIB would recover the costs
from the Ministry of Agriculture for the 80 ha to be irrigated at Kibos 1/
through direct reimbursement and from farmers who would benefit from the
scheme through collection of water charges. At Ahero the scheme is owned by
Government and comes under the responsibility of the National Irrigation
Board. Ahero construction costs would consequently be paid for by Government,
but the costs plus interest would be recovered from farmer beneficiaries in
the form of water charges (Annex 15).
30.       Operation and maintenance in the Chemelil, Muhoroni and Ramisi
zones would be the responsibility, under NIB supervision, of the respective
sugar companies. In the Miwani zone, NIB would operate and maintain the
entire Ahero scheme and the pump station and main canal in the Kibos area.
NIB would arrange to recover all operation and maintenance costs in the form
of a water charge on users (Annex 15).
1/   It is recommended under the Project to reduce the total land area
used for research at Kibos to 40 ha (Annex 6); this area would
be irrigated. The remaining 40 ha of irrigated cane land at Kibos
would be either used by Kibos for commercial cane production together
with the excess rainfed land, or sold.

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Groundwater Investigations at Ramisi
1.        The area around Ramisi lies in the coastal plains and receives be-
tween 1,500-1,750 mm rainfall annually. There are no perennial rivers which
could be used for irrigation. It is therefore necessary to investigate the
use of groundwater for irrigation. So far very little is known about the
groundwater conditions in the area. Five boreholes have been drilled within a
radius of 16 km of Ramisi. The details of these boreholes are given below:
Borehole       Total        Water        Static Water         Tested
Number        Depth        Struck          Level             Yield      Quality
(m)         (m)            (m)              (m /ha)

C 2,791         30.0          5.0             2.5               4.5     Not known
C 3,020         89.3        62.8, 86.9        9.1              13.7     Saline
C 4,167        177.0        18.0, 126.0    13.0                 1.4     Saline
C 3,011         63.4         48.8, 57.9       9.1              45.0     Good
2.        It is intended to carry out a detailed geophysical survey in order
to establish underground structure and conditions to locate fresh groundwater
zones and also to establish the boundary between fresh and saline water.
The geophysical survey would be general, followed by seismic and magnetic
surveys at selected places. The geoeletric survey has to be carried out on
a grid basis and would take ab2ut two months. It is proposed to carry out
the survey over about 20-24 km in order to select four to six borehole sites
for test drilling. The survey, which Government has scheduled to start in
the fall of 1977, would require at least two senior hydrogeologists or
geologists and their crews (one groundwater inspector, four subordinate staff
and two drivers) and two or three four-wheel drive vehicles. It is estimated
that it would take about two months to complete the field work and about the
same time to interpret the results and select the borehole sites.

ANNEX 5
Paae 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Drainage
A. Introduction
1.        The Nyanza Sugar Belt Project area includes a fair proportion of the
flatter parts of the Nyando catchment and is bounded to the north by the Nandi
Escarpment, to the east and south by the Londiani and Kericho highlands, and
by the Kavirondo Gulf of the southwest. The relief of the area may be broadly
divided into (a) the Kano plain, (b) the gently sloping areas below the Nandi
Escarpment and in the Nyando and Ainomotua river valleys, and (c) a steeply
sloping broken area in the upper catchment, highly dissected by rivers and
watercourses.
2         The main river in the area is the Nyando, which at its confluence
with its2major tributary, the Ainomotua, has a catchment area of about
2,900 km . Below the confluence the river meanders at a flat gradient through
the Kano plain, losing its course in the Miruka swamp before entering Lake
Victoria. Increasing degradation of the upper catchment, through indiscri-
minate deforestation and bad agricultural practices, is causing serious
erosion. The rivers consequently carry a high silt load which is deposited
in the aggraded lower reaches of the main watercourse and this, coupled with
an increase in Lake level arising from power production requirements at Owen
Falls, has given rise to periodic serious flooding of the lower Kano plain
and the formation of seasonal and perennial swamps. The situation is
aggravated by the location of the West Kano pilot irrigation scheme which
interfers with the natural drainage line through the Miriu swamp.
B. Present Situation
Nyanza Sugar Belt
3.        The hydrology of the Nyando basin has been studied in some
detail. 1/ The following are some of the conclusions affecting drainage:
1/   The Hydrometeorlogical Survey of the Catchments of Lakes Victoria, Kyonga
and Albert, Vol. IV, Studies of Selected River Basins, WMO 1974.



- 48 -
ANNEX 5
Page 2
(a) The average length of non-channel flow from a point
on the drainage divide to a point on the adjacent
stream channel is 800 m. 1/ The longest flow path
from the basin perimeter to the head of a first
order stream channel is 6 km.
(b) Intense continuous spells of rain do not occur
for more than six hours. Eighty percent of rainfall
spells continue for at least two hours; only 3%
exceed six hours. Eight percent of storms,
precipitating between 10 mm and 60 mm, last less than
two hours. The point precipitation for the maximum
24-hour storm observed was 85 mm (intensity 3.5 mm/hr),
but it was also observed that precipitation for 12-hour
and 24-hour storms was constant, i.e. storms do not
last for more than 12 hours. This compares with the
24-hour rainfall of 70 mm, 90% falling within three
hours, taken by Tate and Lyle as the one-in-five year
return period storm.
(c) Runoff from the catchment varies between 3% and 15% of
the rainfall, depending on the condition of catchment
saturation.
Details of the 24-hour storm area/duration/depth relationships, probable
floods and rainfall/runoff coefficients are shown in Table 1.
4.        In the Sugar Belt present drainage problems arise in the flatter
lands in valley bottoms and chiefly, where cane-growing areas extend into
the Kano plains. These problems are accentuated by the poor internal drainage
of the heavier clay soils in the area: Annex 2, Appendix 2 describes these
soils in detail. To some extent the limited capacity of the main road cul-
verts, as well as those traversing the railway line, act as bottlenecks and
cause limited inundation of adjacent fields. The field area affected by
adverse drainage, measured off Maps IBRD 12992 and IBRD 12993, is 15,030 ha 2/
of which 11,760 ha is in large blocks greater than 100 ha. Table 2 details
drainage areas for each factory zone.
Ramisi
5.        In this zone topography and soils (Annex 2) do not give rise to
serious problems of internal drainage and waterlogging. Improvement of
present conditions could be achieved, essentially, by better agronomic
1/   Based on 50 points uniformly spaced around the basin perimeter.
2/   The Sugar Belt drainage area (150 km ) represents 5% of the catchment
area of the Nyando river and hence can have only a limited effect in
flooding in the Kano plains (para. 19).



- 49 -
ANNEX 5
Page 3
practices and, chiefly, by land forming and land preparation. These factors
are taken into account in Annex 6. Minor drainage has to be provided in
irrigated areas, however (Annex 4).
C. The Project
6.        Design criteria for drainage are based on an estimated 24-hour rain-
fall of 70 mm having an estimated return period of one year in five. Since
few, if any, storms have a duration exceeding 12 hours (Table 1), drains
have been designed with dimensions which would allow 70 mm of rainfall to be
evacuated in 24 hours.  This is equivalent to a dr_.nage module of 8 1/sec/ha.
The assumption is made that fields are saturated at the time of the storm and
that drains flow bank-full.
Project Components
7.        Land preparation would involve land smoothing and the formation
of tertiary drains; 7 m wide cambered beds would be formed for furrow pre-
paration. Furrows and, hence, the cambered beds, would be set out to a
gradient of 0.2% and would not exceed 250 m in length. Land preparation
would be carried out by bulldozer, grader and, if necessary, landplanes after
the end of the five-year cane cycle when the fields would be replowed. At
the same time 500 m long tertiary drains would be formed at 250 m intervals,
perpendicular to the furrow lines. These drains would be vee-drains, 0.65 m
deep and 10.40 m wide (slopes approximately 8:1); set out to a gradient of
0.05%. Their 100 1/sec capacity would be capable of draining 12.5 ha.
8.        Field drains would be constructed at 40 m intervals (every 25 cane
rows) and would discharge into the tertiaries. They would be trapezoidal
in cross-section, 0.60 m top width, 0.50 m bottom width and 0.15 m deep
and would be set out to the same gradient as the furrows. They would be
formed by trencher and the spoil would be spread by hand or by an agri-
cultural tractor mounted with a grader blade. Drain lengths are summarized
in Table 2.
9.        Collector drains, 1,750 m long, would be excavated at 500 m inter-
vals. They would collect the d4schLrge of the tertiaries and would drain
100 -ha blocks. They would be dimensioned to discharge 800 1/sec and be
trapezoidal in section, with side slopes 1.5:1, top width 1.25 m and set out
to a gradient of 0.1%. Excavation would be by hydraulic backhoe ditchers and
the spoil would be spread on the field roads.
10.       Main drains, approximately 3 km long, would drain 400 ha blocks. 1/
They would discharge the outflow from the collectors to natural watercourses.
1/   The area of main drainage blocks could vary according to field layouts.



- 50 -
ANNEX 5
Page 4
Their capacity would be 3.2 m /sec and they would be trapezoidal channels with
1.5:1 side slopes, top width 5.40 m, set out to a gradient of 0.05%. They
would be excavated by hydraulic backhoe ditchers and the spoil would be spread
on the roads alongside which they would generally be aligned. Because of
the drains' alignment alongside roads it is not anticipated that there would
be problems of land acquisition.
11.       Improvement works for main watercourses would involve desilting,
cleaning banks and, where necessary, re-aligning. It has been assumed that
the rehabilitation of natural watercourses would involve 1/3 of the earth-
moving required for the construction of main drains. The work would include
all extra excavation needed to enlarge the drains alongside the main road
and railway line to increase their storage capacity and alleviate the water-
logging of the adjacent fields as discussed in para. 4.
12.       Culverts for field roads would consist of two, 8 m long, 800 mm
diameter concrete pipes laid side by side every 500 m under field roads.
In the large blocks about two larger access road culverts would be required
per block, consisting of four, 10 m long, 1,000 mm diameter concrete pipes
laid side by side.
13.       Topographic Mapping.  The Project would provide for large-scale
topographic mapping of the Nyando catchment area and the Kano plains to
allow for future planning of a regional drainage scheme (para. 20 and
Appendix 1).

E. Organization and Management
17.       There has been no survey or detailed design of drainage works in
the area. It is therefore proposed that the same technical committee formed
within the Kenya Sugar Authority to advise KSA on irrigation implementation,
(Annex 4, para. 28) would be responsible for the implementation of drainage
in the Sugar Belt. The committee would assist KSA in drawing up terms of
reference and in the selection and appointment of consultants for executing
the drainage works. The consultants would examine the hydrology of the basin
and decide on the drainage parameters to be adopted in final designs. They
would carry out all surveys and detailed design works necessary for drawing
up contract documents and calling for tenders. They would also provide site
supervision of the works to be constructed under contract. There are
sufficient experienced local contractors to carry out these works but inter-
national competitive bidding should be used.
18.       The capital costs of works would be borne by Government through the
KSA. Operation and maintenance would be carried out by the sugar companies
using equipment purchased under the Project for maintenance of drainage and
roads. Operation and maintenance would be paid for directly from a levy fund,
administered by KSA, earmarked for the maintenance of sugar roads and drainage
infrastructure (Annex 13, para. 16). The Provincial Chief Engineer, Ministry
of Water Development would be responsible for certifying that drainage main-
tenance had been carried out satisfactorily before any disbursement to the
companies would be made from the fund for drainage maintenance.



- 53 -
ANNEX 5
Page 7
F. Environmental Effects
19.       At present, severe flooding occurs over something between 5,000 and
10,000 ha of the Kano plains annually for reasons described in para. 2. This
flooding is relatively little affected by storm runoff from the Sugar Belt
since the topographical features of the main road and the railway line with
their culverts attenuate, to some extent, the runoff from the Nandi escarp-
ment. Below the railway line, in the cane growing area extending into the
Kano plain, the lack of drainage slows down the runoff. Nevertheless, most
of the runoff from the Sugar Belt reaches the Kano plains sooner or later.
20.       The Project does not propose to alter the rail and road culverts
and bridges to allow more rapid evacuation of storm runoff from the Nandi
Escarpment. The proposals to provide a drainage system for 15,030 ha does,
however, mean that runoff from this area will reach the Miruka and Miriu swamps
more rapidly than at present. The results of the design storm (para. 6) on
the Kano plains, for example, would mean that 10.5 Mcm would be discharged
into the plain in between 24 and 48 hours instead of in something like a week
at present.  If it is assumed that outflow into the lake is negligible during
this period the effect would be to raise the water level in the swamps tempo-
rarily by less than 20 cms, which is acceptable.  The definite effects of the
Sugar Belt drainage on flooding could only be determined in the course of a
detailed study of regional drainage and catchment rehabilitation which ought
to be undertaken in the future and for which topographic mapping is provided
under the Project (para. 13).
December 20, 1977

Drainage
Topographical Mapping of the Kano Plain
Introduction
1.        In order to provide basic information which would allow a regional
drainage and catchment rehabilitation plan to be prepared for the Kano plain
it is proposed to complete large-scale topographical mapping of the area.
This would involve some photogrammetric work on existing, but incomplete,
map sheets, where the planimetric detail, but not the relief, has been
carried out, in addition to some new mapping.
Existing Photography and Mapping
2.        Air photo cover, carried out since 1971, is available at a scale
of 1:12,500, which would be a satisfactory base for the additional mapping
required. About 175 km of the Miwani area, between the railway line and
the Nandi escarpment, has been planimetrically mapped to a scale of 1:2,500,
but no contouring or sp2t heights have been plotted. There is also 1:2,500
mapping of about 300 km of the Chemelil and Muhoroni zones.
Proposal
3.        The area to be mapped would cover about 1,500 km , including the
Kano plain between Lake Victoria and Koru in a West-East direction, and
between the Nandi escarpment and the Kericho highlands in a North-South
direction. Map scale should be 1:5,000. Where slopes are flatter than 5%,
the vertical interval should be 2 m. On steeper slopes the contour interval
should be ; m. Spot heights, correct to 20 cm, at a density of one point to
every 4 cm of plan, should be plotted where slopes are flatter than 2%.
Existing 1:2,500 mapping would have to be redrawn to 1:5,000 scale, including
spot heights and contours.
Cost Estimate
4.        Cost estimates are based on rates quoted by Kenya Survey for the
mapping operations described.

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Rehabilitation and Expansion of Sugarcane Production
A. The Nyanza Sugar Belt
Introduction
1.        The Nyanza Sugar Belt comprises three production zones each centered
on a factory, namely Miwani, Chemelil and Muhoroni. In 1976, the total area
under cane amounted to some 31,000 ha from which just over one million tons
of cane were reaped producing nearly 100,000 t of sugar. Production figures
for the last 10 years show that, although cane and sugar production appear to
have fluctuated fairly considerably in each factory zone, in fact, the total
production for the Sugar Belt, 1972 excepted, has been fairly constant. This
can be attributed largely to a zoning system which does not function properly
(Annex 2). A definite crushing period would be strictly adhered to by all
factories in order to stop movement of cane from one zone to another (Annex 9).
2.        The average production for the Sugar Belt was 33 t of cane per hec-
tare per year (tchy). Extraction, or sugar made as percent of cane, averaged
9.6 and that reflects the poor quality of the cane milled as well as factory
performance. Although the marginal conditions of soil (large acreage of heavy,
poorly-drained soils) and climate (low annual rainfall and low temperatures
due to altitude) affect yields in both cane and sugar, the low yields can be
attributed in a large measure to poor management of the sugarcane plantations.
The standards of husbandry vary considerably and so do the yields. Some good
crops of plant cane yield 100-125 t/ha, while neglected fields yield less than
20 t. It is evident that efficient management and good husbandry involving
particularly weed and disease control, adequate fertilization and drainage
would lead to the production of good crops of cane within the potential limit-
ation of the area. In summary, the main field problems of the Sugar Belt are
the marginal nature of the area, the poor standards of crop husbandry, absence
of proper timing of cane harvest, and lack of definite guidelines for cane
cultivation due to inadequate research and experimentation.
3.        The present situation in the three factory zones of the Sugar Belt
is as follows:
(a) The Miwani Zone has approximately 9,300 ha under cane dis-
tributed as follows: 3,200 ha in the nucleus estate,
3,800 ha under large-scale farms and 2,300 ha belonging



- 59 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2
to the cooperative societies under SBCU. Cane production
amounted, in 1976, to 282,000 t, giving a yield of 30 tchy
over the 9,300 ha of cane land; the yield on the nucleus
estate averaged 35 tchy and in the outgrowers area 28 tchy.
Sugar made as percent of cane stood at 8.6, giving a sugar
production of 25,000 t, well below the rated factory capacity.
(b) The Chemelil Zone covers an area of some 12,500 ha under
cane 3,000 ha of which fall in the nucleus estate and
9,500 ha in the outgrowers area subdivided between coopera-
tive farmers (3,200 ha) and large and small independent
farmers (6,300 ha). Cane production amounted, in 1976, to
456,000 t giving a yield of 36.5 tchy over the 12,500 ha
of cane land; the yield on the nucleus estate averaged
45 tchy and in the outgrowers area 34 tchy. Sugar made as
percent of cane was 10.1, giving a sugar production of
46,000 t, again below the rated capacity of the factory.
(c) The Muhoroni Zone covers an area of some 9,100 ha under cane
distributed as follows: 1,800 ha in the formerly privately-
owned Nyando Estate, 700 ha under large-scale farms, 5,700 ha
in the Sugar Settlement Scheme and 900 ha under small-scale
cooperative farms. Cane production amounted, in 1976, to
274,000 t, giving a yield of 30 tchy over the 9,100 ha of cane
land; the yield on Nyando estate averaged 28 tchy and in the
outgrowers area 31 tchy. Sugar made as percent of cane was
9.5 giving a sugar production of 26,000 t, also well below
the rated factory capacity.
Economic Cropping Period
4.        Taking into account the yields obtained under good management,
a five-year crop cycle made up of one plant cane and two ratoons is
proposed. The average growth curve or plant, first and second ratoon
canes between 15 and 23 months old, obtained for Chemelil, shows that
above 19 months no profitable cane weight increase is obtained. Thus,
as plant cane takes more time to become established than ratoon cane, the
cycle to be adopted for cane should be: plant, 22 months; first ratoon,
18 months; and second ratoon, 18 months. A cycle, with required leeway,
would occupy 60 months; the average proportion harvested annually would be
60%. The monthly mean values of Pol % cane, corrected for 16% fiber, for
the last three years obtained for Miwani, which can be taken as valid for
the whole Sugar Belt, show that there is almost no variation in succrose
% cane right through the year the more so that the cane does not flower.
It follows that cane can be harvested over the 12 months. However, it is
not recommended to harvest the cane during the two very wet months of the
year, April and May, due to the damage caused to both soil and cane stools
by infield operations. It is not recommended to harvest cane between mid
March and mid June even in years when cane yields are high, the harvesting
period should therefore be limited to about 235 working days and in an
average year would be about 210 days.



- 60 -
ANNEX 6
Page 3
Cane Varieties
5.        The variety most extensively grown at present in the Sugar Belt
is C0421 with, as a good second, C0331, and C0617 rapidly gaining favor.
Although C0331 withstands well the adverse conditions encountered when
grown in the heavy, poorly drained soils its succrose content is low. C0421,
on the other hand, has a good succrose content but is adapted to the better
soils of the area. C0617 withstands well waterlogged conditions and is now
being used as a replacement for C0421 and, preferably, to C0331 under such
conditions. Another variety which seems to be adapted to the heavier soils
is N51/211. The advantage it has over C0617, which has a high fiber content,
is that cutters like the variety because it is easy to harvest. One drawback
is the big size of the eyes which are liable to damage, specially after heat
treatment. Another good variety is NC0293; however, being a straight cane
it will have to be planted more closely to overcome weed problems. In the
better drained soils it seems that, at the moment, there is no substitute
for C0421. On irrigated land the leading varieties are NC0293 and B41227.
6.        Several varieties are under test.  The promising varieties seem
to be C0775, B35151 and C0622 which is the product of a cross between C0421
and C0331. Considering the marginal conditions prevailing in the Sugar Belt,
in the long run, the answer to increased production will likely come from
hybrid varieties actually bred and selected in the country.
Cane Field Preparation
7.        The new area to be brought under cane cultivation, which would be
in outgrowers areas, would consist of land either lying fallow or supporting
light vegetation. Land clearing would consist mainly of the operation norm-
ally conducted to destroy cane stubble when replanting cane land. It is
estimated that only 10% of the area would require light bush clearing. After
land clearing, preparaton of all land for cane growing on nucleus estates
and in smallholder areas would be conducted by the Agricultural Department of
the factory in each zone. Large farmers would generally prepare their own
land, but some would be expected to avail themselves of services provided
by the factories. Land preparation includes ripping, ploughing, harrowing
and furrowing. In order to achieve the best conditions for cane growth,
these operations would be done mechanically in both the nucleus estates and
outgrower areas. As it would not only be very difficult to conduct land
preparation during the wet months of April and May but also result in an
inferior tillage, the operation would be conducted over 10 months of the
year only. Deep ploughing is not recommended as the heavy, poorly-drained
soils are earmarked for drainage and adequate ripping to 65 cm followed by
ploughing with the heavy plough would lead to a reasonable tilth for root
development.
8.        In the heavier soils, cross-ripping and ploughing would be done
with the use of crawler tractors, whereas in the lighter soils, four-wheel
drive tractors would be used. Crawler tractors might have to be used for



- 61 -
ANNEX 6
Page 4
ripping, depending upon conditions in the lighter soils. Ploughing would be
done with a heavy Rome plough in the heavier soils and with disc ploughs in
the lighter. Harrowing and furrowing would be performed by wheel tractors
in both types of soil using a Rome harrow and ridger, respectively. As all
the heavy soils are to be drained, planing, using a wheel tractor and land
planer, is necessary between harrowing and furrowing. A spacing of 150 cm
is recommended except in nurseries, where, the cane being harvested at an
earlier age, it would be reduced to 120 cm. With improved field husbandry,
the addition of a third ratoon could prove economic and would be experimented
with.
Cultural Practices
9.        Two major sugarcane diseases in the Sugar Belt being ratoon stunting
and smut (para 14), both of which can be controlled by hot-water treatment of
cuttings, all material would have to come from nurseries. However, as the
hot-water treatment of cuttings may impair germination of the treated setts,
nurseries would be established in two steps. A-nurseries would be planted
with direct heat-treated cuttings and would cover an area representing 1/100th
of the total area to be put under plant cane. The seeding rate would be 10
t/ha to allow for reduced germination and provide planting material for
gapping. The cuttings, obtained from 12-month old cane, would be prepared
with knives, sterilized by scrubbing regularly in a lysol solution or other
appropriate disinfectant, and used to plant directly B-nurseries which should
cover an area equivalent to 1/10th of the total area to be put under plant
cane. The cuttings would be dipped in a fungicide solution (0.5 solution
of Aretan-3), a procedure which should be standard practice for all plantings,
whether nurseries or regular plantations, and planted at the usual rate of
7 t/ha. The cuttings needed for regular plantations would be obtained from
12-month old cane. To the extent possible, the A-nursery would be located
where irrigation facilities are available. The B-nurseries would be estab-
lished throughout the nucleus estate and outgrowers area in order to minimize
the transport cost of the seed cane. 1/ Nurseries would be used once, the
ratoons being included as part of the plantations. Based on an average
planted area of 2,700 ha/year in each of the factory zones, the B-nurseries
would cover 270 ha and the A-nurseries 30 ha. A small treatment plant treat-
ing one ton of cane at a time would be required. Both A- and B-nurseries
would be regularly inspected and all cases of smut rogued before spore dis-
posal has started. Apart from the very dry months of January and February,
planting could be conducted intermittently during the other ten months of
the year taking advantage of the rains. However, with the unpredictable
occurrence of the rains during that period, effective planting time would
not exceed eight months.
10.       Weeds are a major contributing factor to crop reduction in the
Sugar Belt and weeding would be conducted frequently and regularly until
the canopy closes. The most troublesome weeds are conch grass (Digitaria
1/   To simplify the cost analysis, all nurseries have been assumed to be
located in the nucleus estates (Annex 14); this is not a practical
recommendation, however.



- 62 -
ANNEX 6
Page 5
scalarum), sedges such as Cyperus distans, and the partial root parasite
Strega hermonthica. The ideal solution would be a combination of herbicide
application, hand-weeding and inter-row cultivation which has yet to be worked
out. Experiments would be conducted with such herbicides as Asulox 40 and
TCA applied at very late post-emergence, and such combinations as TCA and
Asulox 40, TCA and Actril DS and Asulox 40 and Actril DS. For the moment,
weeds would be controlled by means of two inter-row cultivations with wheel
tractors, mounted with spring tines and ridges for hilling, in cane not more
than one meter high, followed by 3-4 hand weedings. High clearance may be
needed in tall cane.
11.       A proper fertilizer schedule for cane of the Sugar Belt has yet to
be prepared, and this can only be done on the basis of reliable data on soil
analysis and foliar diagnosis (para 42). In the interim, the recommendations
made are based on a broad grouping of soils into the heavier and the lighter
soils, and on average soil levels of phosphorus and potassium; the latter
element does not appear to be deficient. The following tentative average
applications are recommended:
Sulphate of Ammonia            Triple Superphosphate
--------------------- (kg/ha) ---------------
Heavier     Lighter

Irrigated Cane      Sulphate of Ammonia            Triple Superphosphate
(All Soils)       ---------_---------- (kg/ha) ---------------------

1st and Higher

Triple superphosphate would be applied at planting time only and sulphate of
ammonia 3-4 months after planting or harvest depending upon rain. Scums, as
available would be applied before ploughing.
12.       The seed cane would be planted in furrows.  In order to improve
drainage and aeration and economic tillering, a hilling operation would be
conducted by means of which the cane stool is made to grow on a ridge. Hill-
ing also helps to control weeds (para 10). The operation would take place
early in the life of the ratoon in order to avoid damage to the superficial
roots extending in the inter-row.



- 63 -
ANNEX 6
Page 6
13.       All canes would be burned before harvest (Annex 7).  This operation
would facilitate the work of the cane cutters and mechanical loaders. The
harvesting of green cane would be conducted only where a real fire hazard
exists. Sub-soiling with a ripper or a disc-bedder, which would be conducted
three weeks after harvest would open up the soil and sever the old cane root
system at a time when the new tillers are establishing their own roots which
will rapidly grow in the newly turned-up soil. Whiere the cane is harvested
green, trash would be lined in alternate interrows and subsoiling conducted
in the trash-free inter-rows.
Diseases and Pest Control
14.       The sugar industry of Western Kenya is Ln a very fortunate position
in that several of the major diseases affecting cane have never been re-
corded in the area and insect damage is at an insignificant level. The major
diseases present are smut, ratoon stunting, mosaic, leaf scald and pineapple
disease.
15.       Smut disease is caused by a fungus Ustilago scitaminea.  The varie-
ties presently grown are fairly tolerant to the disease. Control should be
in the testing and release of varieties with an acceptable level of resistance,
and roguing of infested stools in the field. The hot-water treatment of cut-
tings controls seed infection but the plants can become re-infected in nur-
series, hence the necessity of detection and removal of all infected stools
at an early stage before the whips have had time to shed their spores.
Roguings must be conducted in both A- and B-nurseries. Eventually, it would
also be desirable to rogue plant cane.
16.       Ratoon stunting disease is a misnomer in that it does not affect
ratoons only; infected cuttings can give rise to diseased, stunted stools in
plant cane.  Infected cuttings can be cured by treatment in hot water at 50C
for two hours, measured from the time of temperature equilibrium in the bath;
transmission can be checked through knife sterilization (Phenol, Lysol,
Zephirol, Iosan). However, as hot-water treatment of cuttings impairs their
germination, this being particularly true with certain varieties, cuttings
would not be heat-treated for direct plantations but only for the establish-
ment of nurseries in two stages (para 9). The C0421 variety is susceptible
while C0331 appears tolerant.
17.       Mosaic is caused by a virus and can seriously reduce yields in
susceptible varieties. The disease can be controlled by planting resistant
varieties only. Leaf scale is caused by the bacterium Xanthomonas albilineans.
Control is effective through the use of resistant varieties only. Pineapple
disease is caused by the fungus Ceratostomella paradoxa, and can be easily
controlled by dipping the cuttings before planting in a solution of an
organomercurial fungicide such as aretan (9.5% solution of Aretan-3). The
minor diseases present are the various leaf spots (brown, ring and eye),



- 64 -
ANNEX 6
Page 7
Pokkal-boeing and red stripe. Insects are at present causing no real
threat to cane in the Sugar Belt. Those present which directly feed on
the cane are both top and stalk borers and army worms, the control of
which is not considered necessary at the moment; the cosmopolitan pink
mealy bug; Perkinsiella leaf hopper, which is a vector of Fiji disease;
and Rophalosiphon aphid, which transmits mosaic.
Areas to be Developed
18.       The cane areas to be developed would be as follows:
Miwani  Chemelil   Muhoroni   Total

Details concerning the distribution of these areas by nucleus estates, large-
scale farms, cooperative societies, settlement farms and other outgrowers as
well as areas to be irrigated and provided with improved drainage are pre-
sented in Table 1.
Cane Yields and Production
19.       The expected yields and production of sugarcane with the new zoning
(Annex 2), including newly-developed areas, have been estimated taking into
account the location of the soils and assuming that all the heavy, poorly
drained soils would be provided with adequate drainage and that the full
input package (e.g. land preparation, fertilization, weeding, etc.) would be
applied. The maximum estimated yield increase with drainage, taking into
account marginal land, was based on actual observations as follows:
Drainage           Other Inputs              Yield
(Fertilizer, Weeding, etc.)
Without               Low                      25
Without               High                     32
With                  High                     40

Page 8
20.       The estimate of yield increases due to irrigation has been derived
from observations at the Ahero Irrigation Scheme. As there would be no check
to growth under irrigation, the cane could be harvested at an early age.
This would allow four crops to be taken during the five-year cycle instead
of three, as follows:
Yield          Growing Period
(tons/ha)           (Months)

As the expected average yield without irrigation would be in the range of
39-49 tchy, the average yield increase due to irrigation would amount to
26-36 tchy. Detailed yield assumptions, by cutting, factory zone and
growing area are presented in Table 2.
21.       The production of sugarcane in the Sugar Belt would reach full
development in Year 11 and stabilize around 569,000 t/year at Miwani,
590,000 t/year at Chemelil, and 620,000 t/year at Muhoroni. Detailed
phasing of production is shown in Tables 3-5. At an assumed extraction
rate of 10.0%, the annual production of sugar would reach 56,900 t at
Miwani, 59,000 t at Chemelil, and 62,000 t at Muhoroni.
Operations and Machinery Implements Requirements
22.       Land to be planted which has been under light bush fallow will
require clearing and ripping to eliminate the roots before cultivation.
These operations would comprise:
(a) Ploughing using a crawler tractor (120-160 hp) and
a Rome plough. Capacity would be 0.52 ha/hr.
(b) Ripping with a crawler tractor (160-180 hp) equipped
with ripper tines to penetrate to about 65 cm. Capacity
would be 0.48 ha/hr.
In abandoned cane fields a deridging operation will be necessary to split
old ridges, an operation which will also uproot the old cane stools. This
would use similar equipment to (a) above. On the lighter soils where compac-
tion has become excessive a ripping operation similar to (b) above would be



- 66 -
ANNEX 6
Page 9
appropriate but would not be effective on the heavy soils. Preparation of
fields for planting would then comprise:
(a) Ploughing would be conducted in the heavier soils with
a crawler tractor (160-180 hp) with a heavy Rome plough
(Rome TRCH 12/56). The capacity per tractor is 0.56 ha/
hour. On the lighter soils, a four-wheel drive tractor
(90-120 hp) with disc plough would be used; the capacity
is 0.36 ha/hour.
(b) One harrowing would be conducted crossways to the direc-
tion of ploughing using in both types of soil a four-
wheel drive tractor (90-120 hp) and a Rome harrow; the
capacity is 0.48 ha/hour.
(c) Planing would be conducted in all land to be drained as
provided in Annex 5.
(d) Furrowing would be the last mechanical field operation,
apart from digging in-field drains (Annex 5), to be
conducted before planting. In both types of soils a
four-wheel drive tractor (65-90 hp) with ridging bodies
would be used; the capacity is 0.64 ha/hour. In the
heavier soils, a four-wheel tractor (90-120 hp) may
have to be used depending on the state of the soil.
23.       Land preparation would be done nine months per year; the two very
wet months would be excluded and time allowed for the land to dry up. With
24 actual working days per month and 12 working hours per day (two shifts
of six hours each), 2,592 hours of land preparation time would be available
per year.
24.       Maintenance of cane fields after planting would consist of weeding,
fertilizing and hilling. The outgrowers who do not possess the necessary
equipment for mechanized operation would carry out weeding and fertilizing
practices themselves manually, and hilling would be done with a hoe at the
same time as weeding. The work would be conducted under the supervision
of the Agricultural Department of the companies. At the nucleus estate and
in properly equipped (large) farms, weeding and hilling would be a combina-
tion of manual and mechanical operations. Herbicide application would be
used when results of experimental work are available (para 42). A wheel
tractor, equipped with spring tines for weeding and ridgers for hilling
would be used in cane not higher than one meter (5 to 6 months old). This
operation could be conducted twice, followed by two to three manual weedings,
depending upon weed incidence, until the cane canopy closes in. In ratoons,
subsoiling, to open up the soil and break up the old roots (para 13) would be
conducted three weeks after harvest followed by three to four weedings. For



- 67 -
ANNEX 6
Page 10
weeding and ridging a four-wheel drive tractor (65-.90 hp), fitted with spring
tines for weeding and ridgers for hilling, would be used; the capacity is
0.64 ha/hour. For subsoiling, a four-wheel drive tractor (90-120 hp), fitted
with ripper or disc bedder, would be used; the capacity is 0.40 ha/hour.
25.       Replanting of cane fields after the last harvest would follow the
same sequence of operations as those used for the initial planting (paras
7-12). However, the cane stubbles left in the soi:L would have to be uprooted,
chopped and incorporated into the soil, and the ridges would have to be
destroyed. For that purpose, a crawler tractor (120-160 hp), fitted with
a Rome plough (TRW 24/28), would be used; the capacity is 0.52 ha/hour.
Labor Requirements
26.       Manual labor would be required for planting, fertilizer application,
gapping and weeding, when this is not done mechanically. In the outgrowers
area, the farmers would carry out these activities in their own plots. The
various practices would require the following labor:
Mandays Required per Hectare
In 3 Crops
(5 Years Cycle)
Planting            20                          20
Fertilizing          2                           8
Gapping              5                           5
Weeding             15 (per weeding)           270 (average of 6 weed-
ings per crop)
Total                                     303
Planting, gapping and fertilizing of the plant crop would be conducted from
March to October, taking advantage of the rains. In the higher altitude
belt, with more favorable rainfall, less restriction would apply. At 24
working days per month and seven effective months out of eight, the total
number of days available for these practices is 168. Fertilizing in the
ratoon crops would be done during 10 months, or 240 days, and weeding during
the entire year or 288 days. An outgrowers labor budget per hectare is
shown in Table 6.

Page 11
B. Ramisi
Introduction
27.       The ecological conditions under which cane is grown on the coast
are very different from those prevailing in the Nyanza Sugar Belt. The cli-
mate is tropical, and the soils are predominantly sandy loams. Rainfall is
less than in Nyanza and the temperature almost constant throughout the year.
Although, on the better soils, fair stands of cane were observed, yields are
generally low as some of the cane is being grown on sandy soils better suited
to coconuts or cashews. There is, however, no lack of land suitable for cane
growing in the vicinity of the factory. The standard of husbandry is lQw and
there is urgent need to embark on a well-planned experimentation program in
the area.
28.       The Ramisi zone has approximately 6,000 ha under cane out of
which about 500 ha of nucleus estate land (at Makongeri) is on very sandy
soils, producing negligible yields. The area under actual cane production
(1977) is, therefore, some 5,500 ha, 4,700 ha in the nucleus estate, 480 ha
under large farms and 320 ha belonging to smallholders. Unlike the Sugar
Belt, annual cane production at Ramisi is rather unstable. Over the five-
year period 1972-76, annual production averaged 126,500 t and ranged from
171,600 t in 1973 to 84,100 t in 1976. The five-year average yield was
23 tchy.
Economic Cropping Period
29.       Conditions being tropical on the coast, a standard cropping cycle
of a plant cane of 14 months and two ratoons of 12 months each would be
adopted. The complete cycle, with required leeway would occupy 41-42 months;
the area harvested annually would average 86-88% of total area under cane.
Harvesting could be conducted right through the year; however, due to heavy
rains, the two wet months of April and May would be excluded, thus leading
to a harvesting/operating period of 260 working days.
Cane Varieties
30.       Of the leading varieties C0421 and C0331, the latter withstands
drought conditions better. It appears that C01007 could be a good substitute
for C0331 under these adverse conditions, and C0997 is performing well in
the better soils. Promising new varieties include Q47, Q68, B54152, DB229/57
and Mex 57-197.
Cane Field Preparation
31.       The new area to be brought under cane (para 37) is made up mainly
of land lying fallow supporting light vegetation. It is estimated that
about 25% of the new area would have to be deforested and the remaining



ANNEX 6
Page 12
75% would require only light bush clearing. After land clearing, preparation
of all land for cane growing would be conducted by the Agricultural Depart-
ment of the Factory. This includes ripping followed by ploughing, harrowing
and furrowing. These operations would be done mechanically in both nucleus
estate and outgrower areas over a period of 10 months, excluding April and
May. Outgrowers would be expected to perform the operations of planting,
gapping, fertilizing and weeding.
Cultural Practices
32.       All planting material would receive the hot-water treatment and
would be planted in A-nurseries to supply B-nurseries before being taken.for
regular plantations. The same replanting procedures as outlined for the
Sugar Belt, including smut roguing, would be followed (para 9). Based on an
average plant area of 2,865 ha per year, the B-nurseries would cover 300 ha
and the A-nurseries 40 ha.
33.       Weedings would be conducted frequently and regularly until the
canopy closes in. The use of weedicides would be investigated in the area,
particularly in young cane. Two inter-row cultivations followed by 3-4
manual weedings would be conducted.
34.       A proper fertilizer schedule has yet to be worked out.  The recom-
mendations made are, therefore, tentative and based on average soil levels
of phosphorous and potassium. The latter element appears to be deficient
and it may be necessary to apply muriate of potash. However, considering
the present cost of fertilizers, such application would be investigated
fully in fertilizer trials. Calcium also appears to be deficient. The
initial rates of fertilizer application would be as follows:
Rainfed Cane                CAN        Triple Superphosphate
(kg/ha)              (kg/ha)
Plant Crop                  350                  250
1st Ratoon                 350
2nd Ratoon                  350
Irrigated Cane
Plant Crop                  500                  250
1st and Higher Ratoons    500
Triple superphosphate would be applied at planting only. Calcium ammonium
nitrate would be given in two applications, six to eight weeks after planting
or ratooning and again five to six months later, as the fertilizer is liable
to be leached; split rates of 150 kg and 200 kg for rainfed cane and 200 kg
and 300 kg for irrigated cane are recommended. Scums would be applied before
ploughing as available.



- 70 -
ANNEX 6
Page 13
35.       The present practice of maintaining a trash blanket which precludes
burning of the trash before harvesting is questionable. It is unlikely to
significantly reduce the rate of moisture loss from the soil surface though
it would maintain a lower temperature in the upper few centimeters of the soil
profile. However, considering the labor shortage for cane cutting, burning
would have been desirable as it facilitates the task for cutters, thus doubling
their output, helps the mechanical loading and also provides cleaner cane for
the factory. Experiments would be conducted on the effects of burning on
yields, with particular reference to ratoon regrowth, to ascertain whether
burning could be permitted in the future. The trash left after cutting in the
absence of burning would be raked in one inter-row out of two so that ripping,
especially after mechanical loaders and infield transport have compacted the
soil, of alternate inter-rows can be conducted in ratoons.
Diseases and Pest Control
36.       The same cane diseases and pests are encountered in all the sugar
zones of Kenya. The same control measures as those recommended for the Sugar
Belt also would be adopted in the Ramisi zone (para 14). Insect problems are
somewhat more important than in Western Kenya in that the sugarcane scale,
Aulacaspis tegalensis is present and may on occasion cause damage. At present,
no economical method of control is known. Beetle borers are also present
whose grubs feed on the underground part of the cane; control is possible
through Aldrin sprays.
Cane Area, Yields and Production
37.       Approximately 5,500 ha of existing cane would be improved, taking
into account that 500 ha of unsuitable nucleus estate land would be excluded,
and 4,140 ha of new cane would be established. Existing irrigation on
370 ha would be improved and new irrigation provided for an additional 400 ha
(Annex 4). Nearly two-thirds of new cane land would be located in smallholder
areas (Table 1).
38.       The expected yield estimates with improved husbandry, based on
available yield data and observations of growing cane in the field, are
40 tchy for the nucleus estate and 32-39 tchy for outgrowers under rainfed
conditions (Table 2). Yield levels of 40 tchy have been achieved in a
number of locations with relative ease, and some fields have been yielding
as much as 70 tchy. As the improvements to the irrigation infrastructure
would be relatively minor, the expected effect on yields would consist of
a modest increase from 50 tchy at present to 70 tchy; with the limited water
available, supplementary irrigation is possible only during the 3-4 driest
months.
39.       Cane production at Ramisi would reach full development in Year 11
and stabilize around 400,000 t per year. At an assumed extraction rate of
9.0%, the annual production of sugar would reach 36,000 t.



- 71 -
ANNEX 6
Page 14
Operations and Machinery Implements Requirements
40.       Development of new land would be mainly under light vegetation
(75%) and would need light bush clearing only. The 25% remaining would
have to be deforested by chain felling and piling. As the movement of
mechanical loaders and infield transport would lead to soil compaction
it is proposed to include ripping, an operation not conducted at present,
in land preparation.
(a) Chain felling would be done with the use of two crawler
tractors and one four-wheel drive tractor (120-160 hp)
with bulldozer blade, two for pulling the chain and one
for assisting in uprooting heavy trees. The capacity
of a unit of three tractors which would vary according
to the standing vegetation is on average estimated at
0.3 ha per hour.
(b) Piling would follow chain felling. This would be done by
crawler tractors or four-wheel drive tractors (120-160 hp)
equipped with rake blade. The average capacity is 0.3 ha
per hour.
(c) The other operations such as ripping (cross), ploughing,
harrowing and furrowing would be conducted as for the
lighter soils of the Sugar Belt (para 22).
Land preparation would be conducted during nine months per year, excluding
the two wet months and additional time to allow the land to dry up and
allowing for the occurance of unpredictable hazards.
Labor Requirements
41.       Manual labor would be required for planting, fertilizer application,
gapping and weeding, when this is not done mechanically. In the outgrowers
area, the farmers would carry out these activities in their own plots. The
various practices would require labor as detailed for the Sugar Belt (para 26).
Additional labor would be required for trash lining at the rate of 10 mandays
per ha (Table 6). Planting, gapping and fertilizing of the plant crop would
be conducted from March to November, taking advantage of the rains. Fertil-
izing in the ratoon crop would be done during 10 months.
C. Agriculture Research
42.       Company Research.  Although the main responsibility for research
work is that of the National Sugar Research Institute at Kibos, it is
essential that close cooperation exists between the Institute and the
sugar companies in order that the experimental work conducted by the latter



- 72 -
ANNEX 6
Page 15
can be coordinated and integrated into a well-balanced research program.
Agricultural research for sugarcane at the company level in all sugar
zones would be conducted by a well-qualified company Agronomist, assisted
by an Assistant Agronomist. The work of the Agronomist and his staff would
include:
(a)  Establishing experimental fields, and collecting and
interpreting the data. Priority would be given to
variety and fertilizer trials.
(b)  Assessing the nutrient status of cane fields through
soil analyses.
(c)  Collecting leaf samples for analysis at the National
Sugar Research Institute.
(d)  Conducting maturity tests in collaboration with the
factory for the harvesting program.
(e)  Testing herbicides after preliminary servicing at the
National Sugar Research Institute.
(f)  Inspecting cane fields for detection of diseases and
pests with particular reference to smut, and roguing
smutted roots in A- and B-nurseries and, eventually,
plant cane.
(g)  Carrying out the hot-water treatment of seed cane.
(h)  Supervising meteorological observations..
(i)  Running the field laboratory for both cane and soil
analysis.
(j)  Advising the Agricultural Manager on all agricultural
matters and on agricultural problems that may arise.
Project costs for company research are included in the costs of the
companies' agricultural departments (Annex 11).
43.       National Research.  The National Sugar Research Institute is one of
the six national research stations for which the Director of Research of the
Ministry of Agriculture is responsible. Considering the important role that
research has to play in the rehabilitation of the sugar industry of Kenya,
considerable strengthening of the present set-up has to be undertaken.
Attention has already been paid to this particular aspect of sugar development
under the South Nyanza project, which provides US$1.2 million for staff and
equipment for Kibos and requires that a comprehensive sugar research program
be prepared by July 1, 1978. However, recent developments suggest that the
strengthening should go even further and include, interalia, the important



- 73 -
ANNEX 6
Page 16
aspect of cane breeding. Conditions in the Kenya sugar lands being marginal
for cane growing, a break-through in cane yields will depend upon the produc-
tion of superior-yielding hybrid canes bred and selected under the environ-
mental conditions prevailing in the country.
44.       At the present time, the Research Institute covers too large
an area (200 ha). Therefore, the size of the station would be reduced to
a manageable 40 ha compatible with the research needs of sugarcane. Irriga-
tion would be provided at Kibos as cane under selection and propagation should
not be subjected to water stress (Annex 4).
45.       The most urgent task the station faces is the development of its
own cane breeding program. This work has already been performed with success
at the Mtwapa Agricultural Station, some 25 miles north of Mombasa, in the
late 1960's before it was transferred as a community project to the breeding
station at Kibaha, Tanzania. The breeding process consists of various variety
selection and disease screening operations which are estimated to take about
twelve years. Good commercial varieties bred elsewhere are imported from
overseas and, after a rigorous closed quarantine at Muguga, followed by open
quarantine at the research stations, subjected to a selection process esti-
mated to cover eight years. The sugarcane breeding station at Mtwapa, where
flowering conditions are good and where it is known that cane breeding can
be conducted successfully, would be reopened under the Project.
46.       The National Sugar Research Institute at Kibos, where environmental
conditions are not conducive to flowering, would be responsible for:
(a) The production, introduction, testing and selection of cane
varieties adopted to the various conditions prevailing in
Kenya sugar lands. Such testing would be properly coordi-
nated between Kibos, the nucleus estates and the outgrowers.
(b) The screening of new sugarcane varieties in disease resistance
trials during the selection program in order to assess their
reaction to major diseases present in Kenya. Susceptibility
to insect damage would be assessed concurrently.
(c) The conduct of cane nutrition studies through soil and leaf
analyses for different varieties in balanced fertilizer trials.
(d) The evaluation of the influence of environmental factors on
cane maturation in order to assess optimum times of harvest
and a balanced crop cycle.
(e) The study of different cultural practices in different soils
with particular reference to tillage/drainage/fertilizer
interactions.



- 74 -
ANNEX 6
Page 17
(f) The economics of irrigation.
(g) The screening of herbicides and the development of balanced
spraying/mechanical/manual treatment programs.
(h) The determination of correct sampling and analytical
methods to determine the succrose content of cane.
47.       It is essential that the closest cooperation be maintained with
the planting community and, to that effect, a liaison unit, headed by an
Agronomist well conversant with sugarcane agronomy, would be created at the
National Sugar Research Institute. The unit would coordinate research and
experimentation at Kibos and each factory zone into a national program, as
a large number of trials will have to be conducted on farmers' land, and
maintain a close liaison with the farming community. This liaison would be
enhanced through diffusion of research findings, organization of seminars,
and training of agricultural personnel of the sugar companies.
48.       In order to conduct the expanded program, the technical personnel
of the National Sugar Research Institute would be strengthened even further
than already provided for under the South Nyanza project. Three more
internationally recruited experts for periods of three to four years would
be needed: a plant breeder, to direct the cane breeding and selection work;
a plant physiologist, to study the maturity behavior and soil-water rela-
tionship of the cane, and weed agronomy; and an agronomist to take charge
of the liason unit and be responsible for the planning and laying out of
experiments on farmers' land, the organization of seminars and the diffusion
of information and training of sugar company personnel. The Kenyan counter-
parts would be provided from existing staff of the Institute. A proposed
organization structure is shown in Chart World Bank 17617, and Project costs
are presented in Annex 11, Table 32. The proposed development of the National
Sugar Research Institute into a national training center for sugarcane
agronomists is described in Annex 8.
