ANNEX 7
Page 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Harvesting, Handling and Transport of Sugarcane
A. Harvesting of Cane
Harvesting Method and Labor Availability
1.        Sugarcane has traditionally been harvested manually in Kenya.  While
there has been ample labor available in western Kenya to do this kind of work,
the local people in the Ramisi area have been unwilling to cut unburnt cane and
laborers have had to be hired from western Kenya to serve as cane cutters at
Ramisi. This arrangement worked well in the past, but signs are beginning to
appear of increasing difficulties in recruiting manpower for cane cutting as
other jobs are slowly becoming available and the pool of labor willing and
able to perform this relatively unpleasant and tiring task is being reduced.
Contributing to this trend is the rapid expansion of the sugar industry in
Nyanza and Western Provinces, which is creating new employment opportunities
both in the construction of new factories and the establishment of large areas
of new cane. In the face of these developments, industry management is in-
clined to substitute mechanical harvesting for the cutting of cane by hand.
2.        Although expensive and from an operational point of view compli-
cated and demanding in terms of required skills, mechanized harvesting is well
developed and could be applied successfully in Kenya. It would most likely
benefit the sugar companies as it would reduce the problems of managing large
numbers of cutting gangs and assure a continous and reliable harvesting capa-
bility. However, mechanization at this time would not be in the national
interest as it is highly capital intensive while the country is suffering
from severe unemployment problems, and the importation of harvesting equip-
ment would contribute to a worsening of the already unfavorable balance of
payments. Measures have to be derived, therefore, to meet the industry's
needs for cane harvesting by other means than full mechanization.
3.        The main thrust of alleviating future shortages of harvest labor
would have to include wider use than at present of mechanical loading prac-
tices and the improvement in living and working conditions of cane cutters.
Manual cane loading, requiring great physical stamina, is most difficult to
recruit labor for and would be replaced by mechanical means under the Project.
In an immediate effort to improve living conditions, the Project would support
construction of additional housing and other social facilities (Annex 10).
To improve working conditions, it would be necessary to first determine the
exact nature of the problem. Such measures as issuance of protective cloth-
ing, the avoidance of the hottest hours of the day in scheduling daily cutting
shifts, the scheduling of optional short-hour shifts, the burning of cane



- 85 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2
before cutting wherever possible, and the institution of a bonus pay system
could be expected, among others, to have a positive impact on labor avail-
ability and should be investigated. To this end, the Ministry of Labor in
cooperation with KSA and representative labor organizations, would conduct a
study and submit recommendations to the Minister of Agriculture and the Bank
before December 31, 1979.
Harvesting in the Nyanza Sugar Belt
4.        The cane harvesting schedule is presented in Annex 6, Table 4.
All cane, including smallholder outgrowers cane but generally excluding cane
from large farmers, would be harvested and transported by the staff of the
agriculture services (or equivalent) section of the companies' Agricultural
Department in each zone. Most large farmers would harvest their own cane,
with the factories scheduling the harvest time. Harvesting would not be
carried out during the very wet months of April and May as infield operation
would cause too much damage to both field and cane stubble. The cane would
be burnt prior to harvesting. As this operation may be hazardous under cer-
tain conditions, the burning of outgrowers fields would be conducted under
the supervision of the companies' staff. In order to avoid deliberate burning
by farmers so as to obtain a quicker return, payment for delivered cane would
be effected taking into account the due date. Burning of standing cane has
the advantage of offering an easier task for the cane cutter as well as
eliminating one field operation which is trash raking after harvest. The
harvesting operation would be conducted by a gang of cutters, 5-7 working
together each along adjacent lines, each cutting a line at a time and, instead
of stacking it, windrowing it on the central row. Depending on cane yield 5-7
lines would be windrowed together to be pushpiled and grabloaded mechanically.
The cutting capacity is estimated at 3 t/day per laborer for burnt cane and
1.0 t/day per laborer for green cane.
B. Handling and Transport of Cane
Existing Cane Handling and Transport
5.        Except for Chemelil, where Cameco SP3000 cane loaders are in use,
the Project factories manually load cane into transport units. Cane is trans-
por.ted by tractor and trailer, trucks or rail to the factories. Because of
the different methods of loading, either crosswise or lengthwise and the
various types and construction of the transportation units several systems for
unloading cane are installed at the factories.
6.        At Miwani, cane is manually loaded into tractor and trailer units,
trucks or narrow gauge rail cars. The trailer units and trucks are unloaded
by gantry cranes or winches and the rail cars are tipped directly into the



- 86 -
ANNEX 7
Page 3
cane carrier. At Chemelil, nucleus estate cane is mechanically loaded into
trailers and bundled with chain slings for unloading by gantry cranes. Out-
growers cane is manually loaded and transported by trailers or trucks. The
units are unloaded by winches or gantry cranes depending on their construc-
tion and method of loading. Muhoroni's cane is manually loaded into trailers
and trucks. Unloading is by a gantry crane or mobile cranes with grabs. At
Ramisi, small bundles of about 20 sticks of cane are manually loaded into
trailers or trucks. The cane bundles are manually unloaded at the factory.
Proposed Cane Handling and Transport System
7.        The harvesting, loading, transport and unloading of cane has to be
developed as coordinated and integrated systems to achieve effective results
in the individual operations.' A standardized system for cane handling and
transport would be established and developed over a period of several years
to replace the various systems presently in use. The existing transport
equipment would be adapted for use in the new system. The basic components
and parameters of the new cane handling and transport system would include:
(i) two 8 hour shift operations to increase utilization of
available equipment instead of the present single shift
operation;
(ii) mechanized cane loading to permit two shift operations,
improve turnaround time of transport units, and release
labor for cane cutting;
(iii) cane piling into windrows for mechanical loading instead
of stacking or bundling to further reduce labor require-
ments for loading;
(iv) chain slings to unload bundled cane from the transport
units;
(v) improvements in the factory cane yard to minimize
queuing time for weighing and unloading; and
(vi) organization and coordination of all cane harvesting,
loading and transport by the factories' Agricultural
Department.
8.        The weighted average one-way cane transport distance for the fac-
tories was taken at 8 km in the Nyanza Sugar Belt and 10 km at Ramisi.  Cane
harvesting would be organized simultaneously in both near and distant fields
so that the available transport equipment would be adequate to handle the
daily factory cane requirements. About 25% of the cane supplies to Miwani
and Chemelil would continue to be transported by large farmers who operate
their own equipment. The availability of cane transport by large farmers



- 87 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4
and others was discounted by 50% to insure that adequate numbers of factory-
owned units would be available in the future. Some private transporters are
expected to leave the cane transport business as the profit margins would be
reduced under the Project because the factories would base their charges to
only recover their costs for the services.
9.        All cane would be mechanically loaded by cane loaders.  Cane would
be transported in new 8 t capacity trailers or existing trailers and trucks
converted if necessary and fitted with chain slings. Winching tractors would
be available to assist the transport units in the fields. The costs of equip-
ment for cane transport and loading are shown in Annex 11, Tables 5-8.
November 1, 1978



- 88 -
ANNEX 8
Page 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Staff Training
1.        In view of the planned expansion of the sugar industry, it is
necessary to create a systematic and comprehensive training program to satisfy
future manpower requirements. The most critical need in the industry is for
training in all aspects of sugarcane production.
Coordination at the National Level
2.        The amended authority for the Kenya Sugar Authority (Annex 1)
includes a new position of Training Officer, financed under the South Nyanza
Sugar project. The duties of this position are to coordinate with Government
Ministries, training officers of sugar companies and institutions of education
and training. Government had agreed under the South Nyanza project that KSA
will prepare a sugar industry training program by December 31, 1978 to meet
the industry's long-term manpower requirements. That project also provides
for overseas training of three students each in business administration and
sugar technology during the period 1977-81.
Formal Training for the Sugar Industry
3.        The agricultural training institutions, i.e. Nairobi University,
Egerton College and the Embu and Bukura Institutes of Agriculture, do not
provide special courses in sugarcane production, and cane is one of many
crops covered in a generalized program for agriculture students. To effect-
ively cater to the special needs of the sugar industry it is proposed under
the Project to establish a training center for sugarcane agronomists at the
National Sugar Research Institute (NSRI), Kibos (Annex 6). A new building
would be constructed at Kibos with six dormitory rooms, a lecture room and
kitchen/dini-ng room facilities, and a vehicle would be provided. Selected
existing senior staff would be sent abroad for training (para 4) and, upon
their return, would formulate and implement a training program together
with the Director of NSRI and the Training Officer of KSA. The program
would basically consist of a course of 3-4 months for 12-15 participants,
given once a year. The participants would be selected from the agricultural
staff of the sugar companies, extension staff of the Ministry of Agriculture,
personnel of the cooperative movement and the staff of Farmer Training
Centers. A fee, covering food and lodging, would be charged. The first
course would be organized in 1980. The cost of constructing the building,
training existing staff and engaging visiting lecturers is included in Annex
11, Table 32. It is anticipated that senior staff from the sugar companies
would actively participate in the training program as guest instructors. The
training center would also be used for extension activities to be undertaken
at Kibos.



- 89 -
ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        The immediate task in developing a local training capability in
Kenya and preparing counterpart staff for expatriate research personnel
is to train Kenyan research scientists in a country with high scientific
standards and where the sugar industry still employs a large labor force.
The University of Mauritius and the Sugar Research Institute in Mauritius,
which offer short-term courses in sugarcane agronomy, would be suitable
institutions for this purpose. The courses would be of 4-6 months duration
and concentrate on sugarcane husbandry and field trial methodology. To
the extent that Mauritius is unable to accommodate the Kenyan requirements,
alternative overseas locations would be chosen in consultation with the
Bank. During the first two years of the overseas agricultural training
program, NSRI would train a plant breeder, a plant pathologist, and an
extension/training agronomist in order to counterpart the expatriate staff
at Kibos and the Mtwapa station. Thereafter, one trainee per year would be
sent for the following three years of the Project, bringing the total NSRI
staff with overseas training to six (Table 1).
Training of Company Staff
5.        The four companies rely on university graduates for potential
senior management staff, who are then given on-the-job training and advance
through experience. Many senior management positions in all companies are
currently filled by expatriates who are supervising and training Kenyan
counterparts. With the proposed expansion under the Project, additional
senior staff would be required in agriculture to manage the outgrowers produc-
tion and improve the companies'agronomic expertise. Few additional positions
would be created in departments other than agriculture.
6.        Agricultural Personnel.  All of the Project sugar companies would
be training additional personnel needed to fill positions in their expanded
Agriculture Departments. The senior agronomist, the nucleus estate manager
and the outgrower manager for each company, i.e. 12 persons including existing
and newly hired staff, would be the first to get overseas training for 4-6
months similar to the NSRI staff (para 4). This training would be phased over
a five-year period and includes 13 additional people for replacements.
7.        Senior field extension superintendents of the Agriculture Depart-
ments would be sent abroad for training, preferably at the CDC Training Center
in Swaziland; a total of 27 candidates would attend a 3-month course over the
6-year period. Another 21 field superintendents would be trained at the Kibos
NSRI training center when the training program starts there in 1980 (para 3).
The costs for overseas and local agricultural training of company personnel
are included in the companies' agricultural component, Annex 11, Tables 9-12.



- 90 -
ANNEX 8
Page 3
8.        Overseas training of agricultural personnel is also required
for other sugar zones in the country. To fill this requirement, the Kenya
Sugar Authority would send a total of seven senior agronomists overseas,
starting in 1979, to participate in the same program as NSRI staff (para 4).
KSA would also sponsor training for field extension personnel in Swaziland
or comparable location; starting in 1979, four trainees per year would be
sent for a period of four years. Project costs are shown in Annex 11,
Table 33.
9.        Factory Personnel.  Some training of factory technicians is current-
ly being undertaken, particularly at Chemelil and Muhoroni where expatriate
managers are training counterpart personnel under the terms of their manage-
ment agreements. In-house training of lower-level personnel is not well
organized but has contributed to raising skill levels to at least minimum
acceptable standards. Factory performance statistics clearly indicate,
however, that the factories operate below internationally accepted standards
of efficiency and that deficiencies in the quality of personnel at all levels
are a major contributing factor to this. The upgrading of factory staff must
be, therefore, a major long-run objective for the companies. The upgrading
process can only be gradual and relatively slow as there are virtually no
other suitable staff resources in the country that the companies could draw on
and, importantly, there are no specialized training programs for factory
operation to which employees can be sent. The companies would thus improve
their training programs within the overall scope of staff development through
organization of more effective internal courses and apprenticeship training,
accompanied by a systematic selection process for training candidates.
Courses to be attended at an Industrial Training Center for vocational
skills would be part of the training programs.
10.       Improvements at the middle and senior management levels in the
factories would be a combination of efforts, including replacing incumbents
with better qualified staff to the limited extent possible, engaging addi-
tional staff where appropriate, providing opportunities for existing staff to
gain experience through visits of sugar factories abroad, and creating per-
formance incentive systems in cooperation with KSA. Proposals for replace-
ments and hiring of new staff would be part of the plan to be prepared by the
companies and submitted to KSA for approval (Annex 9). Major cost implica-
tions are not expected from these proposals. Several key factory staff
members would be selected annually to visit efficiently operated sugar fac-
tories abroad at an estimated cost of KShs 25,000 per staff member. Several
factories would be visited by each group for 3-4 days of discussion in each
factory. Detailed cost estimates are included in the Project's factory
component, shown in Annex 11, Tables 24-27.
11.       The KSA would assist in stimulating performance incentives through
various measures. It would, on a monthly basis, publish key industry statis-
tics for all sugar companies in Kenya. The Project would provide adequate
laboratory facilities for the factories, and standard laboratory procedures
would be adopted. This would enable the companies to provide the required



- 91 -
ANNEX 8
Page 4
statistical information to KSA for publication. Wide exposure of well-
established performance indicators would be an incentive for company manage-
ment to increase their performance. In an effort to achieve better results,
the companies would be able to avail themselves of advisory services pro-
vided by KSA's sugar technologist free of charge.
Training of Cooperative Staff
12.       The Ministry of Cooperatives is providing training for cooperative
union personnel and for society managers. As the main training center, the
Cooperative College, has more applicants then places in its training courses,
it is recommended that short-term training courses for society managers be
conducted in the Sugar Belt. The courses should be for a 2-week period,
covering cooperative organization and management, bookkeeping and credit
supervision.
December 20, 1977

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Rehabilitation and Expansion of Sugar Factories
1.        The factory component of the proposed Project would include the
rehabilitation of existing factories and expansion of production capacity
for three sugar factories. The existing conditions and capacities of the
factories are discussed in Annex 3.
Factory Capacity
2.        The proposed capacities of the Project sugar factories based on
projected annual cane supplies are as follows:
Proposed Capacity of Project Factories
Projected Average Annual   Required Factory         Rated Factory
Factory            Cane Supply        a/       Capacity   b/          Capacity
(tons)                   (TCD)            (TCH) c/ (TCD)
Miwani                 569,000                   2,188             100      2,200
Chemelil               590,000                   2,269             105      2,300
Muhoroni               620,000                   2,385             109      2,400
Ramisi                 402,000                   1,546              70      1,550
a/   From Annex 6.
b/   Based on 260 milling days per year.
c/   Nominal factory capacity, tons cane per hour.
The capacity of the Miwani factory would be increased by 10% to 2,200 tons
canq per day (TCD). The present 2,300 TCD capacity of the Chemelil factory
is adequate to handle its projected cane supply. Muhoroni's factory capacity
would be increased by 20% to 2,400 TCD. The present Ramisi factory capacity
would be increased by 55% to 1,550 TCD. Because the Chemelil factory has
recently been rehabilitated and expanded to 2,300 TCD and is in good condi-
tion, it is not included in the factory component of the Project.



- 94 -
ANNEX 9
Page 2
Milling Season
3.        The proposed milling season covers 44 weeks with eight weeks
during the rainy season as the annual off-season period. Equipment
overhaul and maintenance would be carried out during the off-season
period. A weekly factory shutdown of one day duration is scheduled
for routine cleaning and maintenance and repair of equipment. The
total annual factory operating days would be 260 days based on the
following assumption:
Proposed Milling Season
Annual Gross Time                     52 weeks
Off-Season Period                      8 weeks
Milling Season                        44 weeks
Available Days                       308 days
Scheduled Stops a!                    43 days
Holidays b/                            5 days
Operating Days                       260 days
a/ Based on one day per week for repairs
and maintenance.
b/ Assumes that 50% of holidays would be
arranged as scheduled stops.
The expanded factory capacities and related proposed investments are
based on 260 operating days per year and 22 net milling hours per day.
Manufacturing Process
4.        The double sulphitation process to produce a direct consumption
white sugar would be installed in the three Project factories. With good
process control and operating practices, the factories would be capable
of manufacturing sugar which meets current Kenya sugar standards. The
Miwani and Ramisi factories already use the double sulphitation process.
Muhoroni uses the defecation-melt process and are presently commissioning
equipment for the melt-sulphitation process to produce a high quality white
sugar. It has been assumed that the melt-sulphitation process would not
be retained for the Project; otherwise, new melt-treatment and sugar boiling
equipment would be required to increase production capacity for the pro-
posed factory throughput. The existing equipment has ample capacity to
handle the projected production rate for the double sulphitation process.



- 95 -
ANNEX 9
Page 3
Scope of Rehabilitation and Expansion
5.        The Project's factory component would include (a) rehabilitation
of the existing equipment and facilities to upgrade performance and ensure
long-term operational reliability and (b) expansion to increase production
capacity. The Project costs reflect the current condition of the individual
factories and the scale of their proposed expansions. Most work would be
required for the Ramisi factory. Least work would be required for the
Muhoroni factory which has recently undergone a rehabilitation and expansion
program. Muhoroni's work would be limited to eliminating production bottle-
necks and upgrading ancillary services and facilities. The work requirements
for the Miwani factory would be in between those for Ramisi and Muhoroni.
6.        Cane Receiving and Storage.  Cane unloading for the Project cane
transport units would be standardized to chain slings. Cane bundled with
chain slings would be lifted from the road transport units by gantry cranes.
Gantry cranes would be installed at the three Project factories for handling
chain-slinged cane bundles. With mechanical loading of cane in the fields,
cane would be delivered during two of the three shifts. Adequate cane storage
for the night shift would be accommodated under the gantry cranes.
7.        Miwani's existing gantry structures would be extended and a 10 t
capacity bridge crane would be installed to handle the additional throughput.
The existing rail car receiving and unloading facilities would be rehabili-
tated to handle cane transported from the nucleus estate. Muhoroni's existing
gantry structures would be reinforced and two 10 t capacity travelling bridge
cranes would be installed. A new gantry structure with 10 t capacity travel-
ling bridge crane would be installed over the outside feeder table to free
the two existing mobile cranes for use as mechanical loaders in the field.
Ramisi's existing cane receiving and storage facilities would be replaced
by a gantry with two 10 t capacity travelling bridge cranes and a feeder
table.
8.        Cane Handling and Preparation.  Cane preparation would be improved
at the three Project factories. Miwani's existing gruendler shredder for the
Mirrless tandem would be modified and repowered and the existing cane knives
rehabilitated. Muhoroni's cane preparation would be improved by installing
a heavy duty shredder. Ramisi's existing knife set would be replaced by two
heavy duty knife sets.
9.        Milling Station.  Roller groovings and mill settings would be
designed to maximize extraction performance with the improved cane prepara-
tion and higher imbibition water levels. Imbibition systems would be
rehabilitated to operate at levels of 200% on fiber. Ramisi's bagasse
diffuser would be commissioned and would operate with two pre-extraction
mills and two dewatering mills. The existing fifth mill would be removed
and the steam engine would be replaced by two steam turbine units each
driving two rehabilitated mills.



- 96 -
ANNEX 9
Page 4
10.       Juice Treatment and Evaporation.  Juice from the mills would be
weighed and pumped through juice heaters for primary heating to about 70 C
before passing to continuous juice sulphitors. Juice pH would be raised to
about 9 to 10 by milk of lime and then adjusted with sulphur dioxide gas to
about 7.2 to 7.5 pH. The treated juice would be heated in secondary juice
heaters to 1030C and settled in clarifiers. The clear overflow juice from
the clarifiers would be screened and would pass to the evaporators for con-
centration into syrup. The syrup would be pumped through the syrup sulphitor
where pH would be adjusted to about 6.2 before pumping to the pan storage
tanks. The underflow mud from the clarifiers would be desugared by rotary
vacuum filters and the by-product filter cake would be transported to the
fields for plowing into plant fields.
11.       The bulk of Miwani's and Muhoroni's work for the juice treatment
and evaporation plant would consist of rehabilitating and upgrading existing
equipment and facilities. Ramisi's existing juice treatment and evaporation
plant would substantially be replaced by new equipment. The major items to
be installed include juice and syrup sulphitation equipment, a clarifier,
rotary vacuum filter, juice heater and a quadruple effect evaporator set.
12.       Sugar Boiling and Crystallization.  The three-boiling-system would
be used for sugar boiling. The variations of the system used by the three
factories would be retained because of the existing equipment arrangements
and layouts. Additional low grade crystallizer capacity and modification
of existing crystallizers from air-cooled to water-cooled units would be
accomplished to handle the increased production rates and improve molasses
exhaustion. Muhoroni's low grade crystallizer capacity is ample to handle
the projected throughput. Additional crystallizers would be installed at
Miwani and the existing units would be upgraded to water-cooled units.
Ramisi's sugar boiling and crystallization stations would be improved
substantially by the installation of three vacuum pans and six water-cooled
crystallizers.
13.       Centrifugal Station.  Centrifugal stations would be equipped with
modern automatic batch centrifugals to handle A and B massecuites and con-
tinuous centrifugals for C massecuites. The existing equipment at Miwani
and Muhoroni is adequate for the projected throughput. Two automatic batch
centrifugals would be installed at Ramisi to provide additional capacity
and standby capacity.
14.       Sugar Packing and Storage.  All sugar would be packed in 100 kg
net weight bags. The factories generally have limited storage capacities
in sugar bins and for bagged sugar in the factory. Sugar would be packed
in two or three shifts as necessary and transferred immediately to the
bagged sugar warehouse. Additional bagged sugar warehouse capacity and
molasses storage capacity was not included in the Project because of the
pending studies by KSA. KSA will prepare a plan for improvement of sugar



- 97 -
ANNEX 9
Page 5
marketing and distribution and conduct a study of molasses utilization by
December 31, 1978. 1/ Sugar and molasses production from the Project factor-
ies would not increase significantly until the early 1980's. The existing
sugar storage capacities at their projected throughputs range from four days
at Miwani, 10 days at Ramisi and up to 16 days at Muhoroni. Molasses
storage capacities would be adequate for two days at Ramisi, four days at
Miwani and 10 days at Chemelil.
15.       Ramisi trucks its molasses productin daily to Mombasa.  Additional
transport capacity would be required for Ramisi. Molasses is transported by
rail tank cars from the factories in the Nyanza Sugar Belt to Mombasa for
export. With the completion of the petroleum pipeline between Mombasa and
Nairobi, additional tank cars would be available in Kenya for transporting
molasses.
16.       Steam and Power Generation.  The factories now use substantial
quantities of firewood and fuel oil to supplement bagasse firing in their
boilers. This is partly due to the frequent slowdowns and shutdowns caused
by inadequate cane supplies and poorly organized deliveries. It is also
due to inefficient boiler operations and inadequate equipment. The operations
are characterized by substantial use of cold water, excessive leakage of air,
insufficient bagasse storage, inadequate instrumentation and other defici-
encies which result in low efficiencies.
17.       The Project would include work to improve boiler efficiencies and
reduce supplementary fuel requirements. The factory condensate collection
and storage facilities would be rehabilitated to provide oil-free and sugar-
free condensates for boiler feedwater. Ancillary systems would be rehabil-
itated to allow better combustion-control. Bagasse storage and handling
facilities would be rehabilitated and expanded to provide continuous and
uniform bagasse feed to the boilers. Muhoroni's present steam and power
generation capacity is adequate for the projected production rates. A new
25 t/hr boiler would be installed at Miwani. A new 40 t/hr boiler and a
1,000 kw turbogenerat2r would be installed at Ramisi. Ramisi's new boiler
operating at 21 kg/cm  and the existing 18 t/hr boiler would have ample         2
capacity for the projected throughput to replace the two existing 10.5 kg/cm
boilers.
18.       Ancillary Services and Facilities.  Existing ancillary equipment
wouldcbe rehabilitated to ensure their operational reliability and additional
equipment and facilities would be installed as necessary to handle the pro-
jected throughputs. Laboratory equipment would be provided. The present
factory laboratories are poorly equipped to provide the information necessary
1/   These studies are recommended under the South Nyanza Sugar Project and
Government gave an assurance that the studies would be undertaken with
funds provided under that Project.



- 98 -
ANNEX 9
Page 6
for good factory operations, process control and support of the agricultural
operations. A list of standard laboratory equipment is shown in Table 1.
Cost of Factory Rehabilitation and Expansion
19.       The costs of the proposed factory rehabilitation and expansion are
summarized in Annex 11, Table 21-23. The total cost, including contingencies,
would be KSHs 39.8 million for Miwani, KShs 20.6 million for Muhoroni and
KShs 109.2 million for Ramisi. The costs are based on price estimates for
September 1978. Foreign costs are c.i.f. Mombasa and include a physical
contingency of 10%. A physical contingency of 15% is applied to local costs
because detailed engineering studies were not available for the individual
factories.
Implementation of Factory Rehabilitation and Expansion
20.        The factory rehabilitation and expansion would be implemented over
a period of five years for Miwani and Muhoroni and six years for Ramisi as
shown in Charts World Bank 17566, 17567, and 17568. It would require detailed
organization, engineering and planning for successful implementation. Impor-
tantly, the work would have to be planned and coordinated so that the factory
could continue its production operations. Much of the rehabilitation work
could only be accomplished during the off-season period. While a two-month
off-season period is recommended during normal operation, it is suggested
that harvesting schedules and cane deliveries be organized by the individual
factories during the implementation years to permit a longer off-season period.
The factories presently have adequate capacity to process the annual crop in
a shorter period of time, especially during the early cane development years.
21.        Implementation would be the responsibility of the factories' staff.
They would carry out the detailed design engineering work and submit a plan
for technical approval by KSA before proceeding with procurement and construc-
tion; KSA approval would be a condition of loan disbursement. Funds are in-
cluded in the Project for consultancy services to assist KSA in evaluating
and approving the plans submitted by the individual factories. This would
be in addition to one internationally recruited sugar technologist to be
appointed by KSA by January 1, 1978 under the provisions of the South
Nyanza Project. KSA would be reimbursed by the factories for all expenses
related to dispensing advice on and approving of the factories' detailed
rehabilitation plan. The factory plan would include the following
information:
(i) detailed material and energy balances and flow diagrams
for the factory;
(ii) technical specifications for equipment and materials to
be procured;
(iii) technical specifications for equipment to be rehabilitated;



- 99 -
ANNEX 9
Page 7
(iv) layout drawings of the equipment and facilities;
(v)  detailed schedule for implementing the Project, including
manpower and equipment requirements;
(vi) detailed cost estimates; and
(vii) factory organization for Project implementation.
Procurement would be handled by the individual factories. International
competitive bidding procedures would be used except for replacement parts
and items costing less than US$100,000. The factories would undertake
most of the construction work for the Project with their own personnel.
In some cases, Miwani and Muhoroni might have to use contractors because
of time constraints or the special nature of the work. Ramisi would depend
somewhat more on contractors because of the magnitude of the projected work
load.
Factory Staffing
22.       Staffing at the Project factories is adequate and would be main-
tained at current levels. The higher production rates would not require new
workmen.  In some instances, workmen would be reassigned to other jobs.
Training of production personnel to operate new equipment and process would
be carried out by the factory staff. The workmen all have sugar factory
experience and adapting to the new equipment and processes would be easier
than training inexperienced personnel (Annex 8).
Environmental Impact and Safety
23.       The Project sugar factories are presently installing facilities
to comply with Government regulations for factory effluents. The plans for
the the treatment plants which usually consist of a series of ponds must be
approved by an officer of the Ministry of Health before construction. The
treated water is used for irrigation or is returned to rivers. Health
officers periodically take samples and advise the factory of the results.
24.       The Factories Act covers safety, health and welfare requirements
for workmen in Kenya sugar factories. The Act is enforced by the Chief
Inspector of Factories in the Ministry of Labor. Accidents and injuries
in the factories are reported monthly to the Office of the Chief Inspector.
The rehabilitation and expansion work in the Project factories would comply
with the requirements of the Factories Act. An assurance would be obtained
at negotiations that Project work would be implemented and facilities and
equipment operated with due concern for environmental and safety factors.
December 20, 1977

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Roads, Housing and Other Facilities
A. Roads
1.        Since 1960 the Government of Kenya has carried out a staged program
of road development with Bank/IDA and other international assistance. The
South Nyanza Sugar Project includes a road component amounting to US$9 million.
Two of the previous loans 1/ covered the construction of two phases of main
sugar roads and of feeder and field-access roads, and a third supported
the construction of roads at Mumias. 2/ Another Credit 3/ was designed to
assist the Ministry of Works (MOW) in assuring highway maintenance.
Present Situation
2.        Nyanza Sugar Belt.  The tarmac surfaced primary road, C-34, 61 km
long, links the international trunk road, A-1, (Kisii-Kisumu-Kakamega) with
the national trunk road, B1 (Ahero-Kericho-Senetwet Bridge). This road passes
through the Sugar Belt, parallel to the Kisumu-Nairobi railway line, and is
the main road access to the three sugar factories in the Sugar Belt. In
addition, there are 203 km of MOW gravel surfaced (primary, secondary and
minor) Class C, D and E roads and 693 km of Class S (farm-to-mill sugarcane
transport) roads. Of these, 302 km are major feeder roads and the remainder
are field access tracks. In addition, there are 119 km of unclassified roads
on the three nucleus estates, 134 km on large farms and 55 km in smallholder
areas. These are mostly unsurfaced, dirt roads. The Class S roads are gravel
surfaced with a 5.5 m running course, 1.25 m shoulders and (theoretically)
2.75 m wide drains. Upkeep of all roads has been poor because the MOW Pro-
vincial Roads Department maintenance budget is insufficient to keep existing
maintenance equipment operating or to allow the hire of plant from contractors.
1/   1967 Credit 104-KE, Sugar Road Engineering and Construction
1969 Loan 639-KE, Third Highway Project.
2/   1973 Loan 141a-KE, Fifth Highway Project.
3/   1970 Credit 224-KE, Strengthening and Improvement of Highway Maintenance.



- 102 -
ANNEX 10
Page 2
3.        Ramisi.  The international tarmac surfaced trunk road A-14 (Mombasa-
Tanga) traverses the area and, west and north of the nucleus estate, there is
a gravel surfaced primary road, C-108 (Mrima-Kikoneni-Galu). Several Class D
and E roads link the two main roads. No classified roads have been con-
structed in the area specifically for cane transport. Upkeep of the Class D
and E roads has been poor and the surface of these roads is generally bad.
Project Proposals
4.        The Project components for roads would comprise tarmac surfacing
of some existing Class D and E roads and of factory entrance roads; up-
grading of Class D, E, and major Class S roads and of unclassified roads
in smallholder areas; new construction of unclassified feeder and access
roads and access tracks; and provision of road maintenance plant.
5.        Tarmac surfacing would require scarifying, shaping and compacting
the existing 5.5 m carriageway and 1.25 m shoulders and reforming the existing
drains. The surface would then be prlpared and a cutback bitumen prime coat
applied at a rate of 0.85 to 1.10 1/m . After brushing, two seal coats f
80/100 penetration bitumen would be applied at a rate of 1.0 to 1.67 lm m
Twenty mm (finished thickness) of chippings, applied at a rate of 59 m /m ,
would be rolled into the first coat and 12 mm (finished), applied at a rate of
83 m /m , would be rolled into the second.  The shoulders would be sealed with
a hot bituminous seal into which 3 mm nominal size chippings would be rolled.
Factory entrance roads, except at Ramisi, would be reconstructed to MOW
standard bitumen II (2).
6.        Upgrading all roads would consist of scarifying, shaping and com-
pacting the existing carriageway, widening to 5.5 m where required and
reforming or excavating 2.5 m vee drains, 0.5 m deep, outside the 1.25 m
shoulders. Fifteen cm (finished thickness) of compacted gravel running
course would be placed on the reformed sub-base.
7.        New gravel roads would be constructed to MOW gravel road standards
(Table 1) with a 5.5 m carriageway, 1.25 m shoulders, and drains as described
in para. 6. The sub-base would consist of 20 cm of compacted random material
and the running course of 15 cm of compacted gravel. Bridges and culverts
would be constructed to a loading criterion of 2/3 B5 153 HA loading.
8.        Access Tracks would be constructed in the outgrowers area, where
necessary, by opening up the track with a bulldozer, shaping up the formation
with a motor grader and forming vee and miter drains.

Project Costs
10.       Project capital costs are based on an analysis of contract rates
made by MOW Roads Department and issued in 1976. The rates have been updated
to September 1978 (Table 2). Total costs, excluding price contingencies, are
estimated at KShs. 60.8 million (Table 3). Detailed estimates are given in
Annex 11, Table 29.

Tarmac surfaced roads would be resealed every year. Gravel road maintenance
would consist annually of patching and grading plus a second round of patch-
ing and grading with compaction. Roads would be regravelled every fourth
year. Total annual maintenance costs at full development (Year 7) would
amount to KShs 14.7 million, excluding price contingencies, of which
KShs 2.9 million would be incremental costs resulting from the Project
(Table 5).
Organization and Management
12.       Road works to be executed under the Project would be the overall
responsibility of the MOW Roads Department. Since the Department's con-
struction units are fully committed at present the works would be carried
out under contracts let by the MOW. The Department would either carry
out the necessary surveys and designs, prepare tender documents, and supervise
construction using its own staff or, which is more likely, would appoint
consultants for the purpose. To synchronize road construction with agricul-
tural development and establish maintenance expertise early on, MOW would
delegate responsibility for the construction and upgrading of factory entrance
roads, access tracks and all other unclassified roads in smallholder areas
(KShs. 22.6 million, excluding price contingencies) to the sugar companies.
The companies would undertake some construction with equipment provided under
the Project (para. 14) and contract for the remainder with local contractors.
13.       Procurement of equipment for the sugar companies (primarily for
maintenance) would be by international competitive bidding. There are suffi-
cient experienced local contractors to carry out the road works required but
international competitive bidding procedures would be used in letting tenders
for major contracts. Minor contracts under the responsibility of the sugar
companies would be advertised locally.
14.       The MOW Roads Department is unable to maintain the roads in the
sugar zones satisfactorily. Therefore, maintenance would in the future be
carried out by sugar companies using equipment purchased under the Project
for this purpose (Table 6). Each factory would be responsible to the MOW
Provincial Engineer for maintaining the roads in its zone. Maintenance
would be paid for directly from a levy fund administered by the KSA (Annex 7,
para. 18 and Annex 16). The MOW Provincial Engineer would be responsible for
certifying that road maintenance had been carried out satisfactorily before
any disbursement would be made to the factories from the levy fund.
15.       The capital cost of works for all public roads would be borne by
Government. The cost of private roads on large farms and the nucleus estates
would be borne by the respective owners.



- 105 -
ANNEX 10
Page 5
B. Housing and other Facilities
16.       Additional staff housing would be required to (a) accommodate addi-
tional staff to be employed under the Project, (b) eliminate the shortage of
housing that presently exists, and (c) replace some inadequate existing units.
Provision of housing by the sugar companies is required under Kenyan law and
would be essential for economic and social reasons to enable the companies to
attract and retain the required factory and field labor. It has been increas-
ingly difficult to hire cane cutters, particularly in Ramisi, and inadequate
housing has been one of the main reasons.
17.       The Project would provide a total of 3,221 housing units of seven
different standards (Table 7). In the Sugar Belt the primary demand for
additional housing would arise from the companies' need to engage more perma-
nent field staff, except in Chemelil where there is a housing backlog for
semi-skilled staff. Of the total of 1,465 units proposed for Miwani,
Chemelil and Muhoroni, 74% would be Type F houses, the lowest permissible
standard for permanent employees. At Ramisi, unlike the Sugar Belt, a large
proportion of that company's casual field labor has to be hired under con-
tract from other parts of the country, relocated and temporarily housed. The
employment contracts are for several months to a year and are generally renew-
able. For this type of labor it is proposed to establish 1,680 low-standard
units to be distributed over a number of labor camps. The lower standard
would be acceptable as laborers generally would not live there permanently
and frequently have no dependents residing with them.
18.       The entire housing stock would be owned and maintained by the sugar
companies. A site-and-services approach to construction is not recommended
because of the legal implications and established practice concerning housing
for permanent company staff and, additionally, the transient type of laborers
to be accommodated at Ramisi. All housing units would be provided with
streets, lighting and water and sewerage facilities; furnishings would be
included for the higher-grade houses.
19.       In addition to staff housing the companies would construct other
buildings and facilities. A dispensary would be provided for Muhoroni and
Ramisi; a workers club and multi-purpose building for Muhoroni; and office
buildings, garages and storage and miscellaneous structures for Miwani,
Muhoroni and Ramisi.
20.       The total cost of housing and other buildings would be KShs 107
million (US$12.8 million), including price contingencies (Table 8). The
sugar companies would be responsible for implementation. Architectural
consulting firms would be appointed, either individually or jointly, by the
companies under terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank/IDA to carry out
detailed design work and supervise construction. International competitive
bidding procedures would be used in awarding contracts for construction. To
the extent that this would be economically feasible contracts would be sub-



- 106 -
ANNEX 10
Page 6
divided and separately tendered for to enable a larger number of local
contractors to bid. The phasing of construction during Project Years 1-4
and detailed cost estimates are shown in Annex 11, Table 31.
July 7, 1978

ANNEX 11
Appendix 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Project Costs
Contingencies
The Project Time Frame
1.        The calculations and phasing in the Appraisal Report are based
on calendar years; the Project period is 1979-84. All baseline costs are
up to date as of September 1978.
Physical Contingencies
2.        A 10% contingency has been allowed on all Project costs, except
on irrigation and drainage costs where a 35% contingency was applied because
the final engineering designs have not yet been prepared.
Price Contingencies
3.        Price contingency allowances have been applied to all Project
costs. The allowances are in accordance with World Bank guidelines on the
projected international inflation pattern, supplemented by the mission's
estimates as follows:
Foreign Costs                      1978     1979      1980-83
Civil works                         8        7.5         7
Equipment (including inputs)         7       6.5         6
Technical Assistance                 7       6.5         6
Local Costs                         10       9           8
November 6, 1978

KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Proposed Organization
A. Organization of Cane Production
Basic Concept
1.        In each production zone the zonal sugar company would be the central
decision-making body for all matters concerning the production, harvesting,
transport and processing of sugarcane. All cane produced on the nucleus
estate, large farms and smallholder farms within each zone would be supplied
to the zone's sugar company. The zone boundaries, fixed administratively by
KSA, would be reviewed periodically or upon request by any of the sugar com-
panies in consultation with all parties concerned. Production arrangements
would be clearly defined in a cane supply contract between the zonal sugar
company and each individual cane producer. The company would be adequately
staffed, equipped and financed to meet its obligations as a service and
management organization under the terms of the cane supply contract. Cane
producers could contract with the company either as unaffiliated individual
farmers or through a cooperative organization which, together with the farmer,
would become party to the contract. Generally, a cooperative union would
represent the members of its affiliated societies. If a society is un-
affiliated with a union, the society would represent its members. The effi-
cient delivery of services to small farmers would depend upon effective coor-
dination to be provided by the societies and unions.
2.        The Kenya Sugar Authority would act as a regulatory, coordinating
and monitoring agency to create the necessary legal framework and provide
the first level of arbitration. All other organizations and institutions
presently involved in cane production would either cease to do so or coor-
dinate their activities with the zonal sugar company or the cooperative
system; involvement in activities other than cane production would be con-
tinued as appropriate. A formal mechanism would be established to improve
communications and coordination (para. 14). This basic organizational concept
would be applied equally in the Sugar Belt and at Ramisi.
Institutions and Functions
3.        The Sugar Companies.  Each sugar company would have an agricultural
department which would be responsible for the planning and coordination of all
activities related to cane production within the zone. The department would
be staffed and equipped to operate the company's own nucleus estate and
provide services to outgrowers as required. Each grower within the zone would



- 157 -
ANNEX 13
Page 2
be re6-stered with the company. In addition, growers would be required to
enter into a cane supply contract with the company if either (a) a grower's
annual cane production exceeds 20 tons, or (b) the grower expects to avail
himself of any of the company's services. The terms of the contract for large
outgrowers would generally be in the nature of agreeing to supply and receive,
respectively, a certain approximate quantity of cane under specified con-
ditions, with only a limited amount of services to be provided by the company.
In contrast, smallholder outgrowers would receive a wide array of services.
4.        Smallholder outgrowers would be required, if their own plots are
too small, to form together with other farmers blocks of land of sufficient
size for efficient production of cane; unless company management determines
otherwise, a 10 ha block is considered to be the minimum size.  However,
boundaries within each block would be marked and identity of ownerhip of the
cane preserved until harvest time. To be accepted, the land would have to
be suitable for cane production as judged according to preestablished criteria
based on expected yields (Annex 6). Outgrowers would be obligated to partici-
pate in the production of their cane by clearing new land where this can be
done manually and performing the function of planting, fertilizing, weeding
and other functions as agreed according to the company's instructions. The
company would prepare the land for planting; provide treated seedcane,
fertilizer and other inputs as required; furnish extension advice; and harvest
the cane and transport it to the factory. The outgrower and his family would
have the right to be employed by the company to harvest his cane.
5.        Over the next 5-10 years most of these operations would be financed
by the company through an outgrowers loan. The company would establish a
loan account for each outgrower and enter all charges for services and inputs
rendered, including interest at the rate of at least 11% per annum and over-
head. The principal and interest amounts of the loan would be deducted from
the company's payment to the outgrower for cane delivered to the factory in
such a manner as to spread the burden of repayment reasonably evenly over the
initial, and subsequent ratoon, harvests.
6.        Upon request, the company would also provide services and inputs
to outgrowers on a cash rather than credit basis. This arrangement, which
would also be formalized in a cane supply contract, would apply to growers
having access to alternative sources of financing such as commercial banks,
AFC or the Cooperative Production Credit Scheme (CPCS). Customarily, the
financing agencies in this case arrange with the sugar companies for the
collection of their loans at the time of delivery of the borrower's cane
to the factory. The same conditions concerning block size and farmer
participation would apply as for company-financed growers.
7.        The amounts the company would charge for the goods and services
provided to outgrowers would be set at levels estimated to ensure that the
aggregate of such amounts for any year does not exceed the costs to the
company of providing such goods and services. The company would operate
an adequate cost accounting system to determine the costs of its operations
and establish appropriate charges. Within four months from the end of each



- 158 -
ANNEX 13
Page 3
of its fiscal years, the company would furnish an annual report to KSA cover-
ing (a) the estimates made for the past fiscal year of the costs of its
outgrower-related operations, (b) the amounts actually charged outgrowers
during that year, (c) the company's current costs of those operations and
(d) proposed adjustments of current charges and tentative proposals for the
following year. The cooperative organizations would be given an opportunity
to comment on the proposals. In case of disagreement KSA would arrange
for negotiations between the zonal sugar company, cooperative union and
representatives of farmers who are not members of cooperative societies,
and act as an arbitrator if necessary.
8.        The Cooperative Unions and Societies.  These would represent their
members on all matters pertaining to cane production vis-a-vis the sugar
companies and KSA. The unions presently in existence are the Sugar Belt Co-
operative Union (SBCU) and the Muhoroni Cooperative Union (MCU). Unaffiliated
societies include the Nyanza Farmers Cooperative in the Sugar Belt and the
Cane Producers Cooperative at Ramisi. Each union or society would have had
its accounts up to the last fiscal year audited and submitted to the Registrar
of Cooperatives before it could enter into agreements with the sugar companies
or represent farmers under the Project. The unions would be consulted on all
major matters concerning outgrower cane production.
9.        The sugar companies would inform the unions and unaffiliated soci-
eties of the cane development plans in their respective zones and establish a
working relationship to assure smooth implementation. Farmers would in turn
be informed through regular cooperative channels. The unions/societies,
assisted by technical personnel from the sugar companies, would prepare
farmers to become contract cane growers for the companies, select and block
farmers' land and assist farmers and monitor the companies to assure that both
parties meet their obligations under the cane supply contract. The unions
also would continue to administer the CPCS and the Cooperative Thrift Scheme.
Under CPCS, which is financed by the Cooperative Bank of Kenya and would not
receive any Project funds, the unions would approve loan applications, arrange
for a cane supply contract and loan collection agreement with the factory,
assist with the supply of inputs and services where these are not supplied by
the sugar company (e.g. by farmer's choice or in case of ineligibility on
account of insufficient block size, etc.). However, a cane supply contract
with full services and financing provided by the sugar company rather than
CPCS would be the predominant arrangement initially and CPCS would be promoted
only gradually from its present very small scale. The rate of expansion of
CPCS would be determined by farmers' willingness to switch, in consultation
with the sugar companies and the unions, from company financing to CPCS
financing.
10.       All outstanding debts related to SBCU's past cane development
activities on behalf of Government would be transferred to the Ministry of
Agriculture. Maximum repayment of the debt portion owed by farmers would be
sought in cooperation with SBCU and the sugar companies. The portion of
SBCU's debt that cannot be allocated to farmers as well as uncollectable loans
to farmers would be written off. The Ministry of Cooperatives would undertake
to provide the necessary assistance to the unions/societies to enable them to



- 159 -
ANNEX 13
Page 4
complete their financial statements and submit them for a timely audit;
cooperative staff would be trained for their future functions as promoters of
outgrower cane production. The societies and unions would be reorganized into
viable entities where necessary.
11.       The Sugar Settlement Scheme.  The Sugar Settlement Organization
(SSO) in Muhoroni (and Chemelil after re-zoning) would be responsible ex-
clusively for settlement aspects and relinquish all agricultural functions
to the Muhoroni and Chemelil sugar companies and the cooperative unions and
societies. The SSO's primary objectives would be to establish a viable
settlement structure and to collect its outstanding debts from settlement
farmers.
12.       Steps would be taken by SSO to alleviate the problem of absentee
owners. It would carry out a census to determine exactly the degree and
nature of absenteeism in the scheme; it would also prepare a detailed plan
for allocating both the responsibility and the costs of cultivating the plots
owned by these absentees. One proposal to be considered would be the recom-
mendation that the sugar companies operate these farms on behalf of the
owners, charging them for all services rendered. It is recognized that such a
solution would be inefficient, as the companies would have to schedule crop
maintenance activities (especially weeding) on a large number of dispersed
plots. It would also impose higher credit risks on the companies as, in
practice, absentee owners cannot be made liable in case their accounts show a
loss at the completion of the crop cycle, and the companies would have to ab-
sorb the losses. Financial losses are more likely to be incurred on absentee
owners' plots due to the substitution of wage labor for unpaid farm family
labor.
13.       The Kenya Sugar Authority.  The KSA would not be directly involved
in company-outgrower production arrangements but would provide advice and
guidance and assume the regulatory fuctions vested in it. It would resolve
disputes as an arbitrator and assist the sugar companies and outgrower
organizations in dealings with other Government bodies. The Chief Executive
of KSA would be the chairman of the Sugar Belt Coordinating Committee (SBCC)
which would be established as the formal mechanism to improve communications
and coordination; other members would include each of the company General
Managers, the Executive Controller of the Sugar Settlement Organization, the
General Manager of SBCU and MCU, the chairman of Nyanza Farmers Cooperative
and others as may be required. The committee would meet regularly to review
problems, plan courses of action and monitor progress.
B. Infrastructure Maintenance
14.       To assure adequate maintenance of and equitable distribution of
maintenance costs for sugar roads and the drainage works a new system would
be adopted. The sugar companies would be equipped and staffed to maintain



- 160 -
ANNEX 13
Page 5
all roads, except tarmac roads, and major drainage infrastructure in their
respective zones. Infield drains would be maintained by the owners of the
land. The Ministry of Works and Ministry of Water Development would set
maintenance standards for roads and drainage, respectively, and certify
regularly that maintenance operations carried out by the companies are
adequate. The companies would be reimbursed for their expenditures related
to roads and drainage maintenance from a fund operated by KSA. This fund,
henceforth referred to as Roads and Drainage Maintenance Fund (RDMF), would
be financed from a levy on sugar to be collected by KNTC on behalf of KSA.
The exact amount of the levy would be determined annually by KSA, based on
actual maintenance costs during the previous year and adjusted for current
price increases. The levy would not represent a new tax on sugar but rather
be deducted from existing taxes and levies (Annex 14). It has been assumed
in the evaluation that the RDMF would be operational by the time the first
maintenance expenditures are due under the Project in 1980. A tentative cal-
culation of the RDMF levy is included in Annex 15.
C. Organization of the Sugar Companies
The Proposed Organization Structure
15.       The proposed organization of the sugar companies consists of four
departments, each headed by a department manager who would report to the
general manager. These departments include the Agricultural, Factory, Finance
and Controller's and the Industrial Relations Department (Chart World Bank
17748). The new organization would be adapted to the organization presently
existing in each company and modified where necessary (para. 22). At Miwani,
Muhoroni and Ramisi the traditional management system would be modernized
by decentralizing authority and delegating more responsibility to lower-level
management personnel.
16.       The Agricultural Department.  This department would consist of the
Nucleus Estate Divsion, Outgrowers Division, Agricultural Services Division
and a research staff unit. The Nucleus Estate Division would be responsible
for the management and operation of the company's nucleus estate. The Out-
growers Division would plan and execute all activities in the outgrowers area
of the company zone and operate the company's extension service. The Agricul-
tural Services Division would provide all required technical and machinery
services and also be responsible for the maintenance of roads and drainage
works (para. 15). The research staff would conduct field trials and liaise
with the National Sugar Research Institute to obtain the best technology for
field-testing in the zone and extension to the growers.
17.       The expanded operations and the centralization of planning, sched-
uling and implementation of agricultural operations would greatly increase the
management requirement for a continuous flow of specific and timely informa-
tion on field operations. To meet this requirement, each company would



- 161 -
ANNEX 13
Page 6
establish a management information system for its agricultural department.
KSA would assist the companies in this effort with its own staff and, if
necessary, contract a management consulting firm.
18.       The Factory Department.  Divided into two operating and a control
division, this department would be in charge of cane processing. The
Engineering Division would be responsible for mechanical juice extraction
and for factory maintenance, and the Processing Division for the processing
of the juice and for the company's water supply. The Control Division
would monitor sugar quality and perform all required laboratory tests and
control measurements.
19.       The Finance and Controller's Department.  The department's General
Accounts Division and Cost Accounts Division would conduct the company's
central bookkeeping and cost accounting operations. The Materials and Plan-
ning Division would be responsible for the orderly supply and procurement of
all physical inputs and materials. The Finance Section would be concerned
with the company's financial requirements such as borrowing, lending, invest-
ing; capital structure and dividends; taxation; and financial aspects of
contracts. Two staff units would be engaged in budgeting and internal audit-
ing, respectively.
20.       The Industrial Relations Department.  Besides the Personnel Division
there would be a Social Welfare Division responsible for the company's medical
program, housing, etc. and a General Services Division responsible for securi-
ty, cleaning, etc. Two staff units would cover staff recruitment and training,
and salaries administration, respectively.
Company Reorganization
21.       Miwani.  The company's reorganization would involve the establish-
ment of a separate factory Control Division and Roads and Drainage Maintenance
Unit and Survey Unit; and staff units for budgeting, auditing, salaries
administration and agronomic research. Rearrangement of existing staff and
functions would be required to form the Outgrowers Division, Social Welfare
Division, General Services Division and Materials and Planning Division;
Finance Section, Workshop Section, Harvesting and Transport Section and Land
Preparation Section.
22.       Chemelil.  The relatively minor changes required would involve the
setting-up of a new factory Control Division, Roads and Drainage Maintenance
Unit, Survey Unit and staff units for auditing and salaries administration.
The staff and functions would be reorganized in the areas of factory control,
cost accounting, finance, budgeting, materials supply and planning, social
welfare, training and recruitment, general services, and cane harvesting and
transport.



- 162 -
ANNEX 13
Page 7
23.       Muhoroni.  An entirely new Agricultural Department would be
formed; the existing Nyando Estate management unit would be transformed
into the Nucleus Estate Division. Also added would be the Cost Accounting
Division, the Materials and Planning Division, and the staff units for
budgeting, auditing and salaries administration. The social welfare and
general services staff would be reorganized.
24.       Ramisi.  The company would require a major reorganization.  New
organizational entitities to be created would be the factory Control Division,
Cost Accounting Division, Materials and Planning Division, Outgrowers Division,
Roads Maintenance Unit, Survey Unit, and staff units for auditing, recruitment
and training, salaries administration and research. The remaining features
of the recommended organization would be established through reorganization
of the existing nine departments into the proposed four.
D. Management and Staffing
25.       The management agreements with Bookers at Chemelil, Mehta at
Muhoroni and EMCO (Kenya) Ltd. at Ramisi would continue to remain in force.
The Bank/IDA would be consulted in renegotiating these agreements when they
expire or in negotiating new agreements. The implementation of the Project
would be carried out under the terms of the existing agreements and no extra
compensation would be needed as the managing agents would benefit automatic-
ally from increased sugar production through a variable commission clause
already written into the agreements. Miwani would continue to provide its own
management and no outside management assistance is envisaged.
26.       Lower-level factory staff employment had built up in the past
under social pressures to levels which are too high for present operations
and would generally be sufficient to cope with the expanded production
resulting from the Project. Some additional key staff would be needed at
the management and technical levels, particularly in the companies' Finance
and Controller's Department and Agricultural Department. Whereas the entire
Agricultural Department would have to be staffed up at Muhoroni, only the
Outgrowers Division and research units would require staff additions in the
other companies. With the exception of a total of 3 expatriates, company
staff would be recruited locally. Staff training would be provided for
skilled staff at all levels (Annex 8).
27.       Agricultural labor requirements would increase as a result of the
Project. To alleviate potential shortages in cane harvest labor the Ministry
of Labor would review the market for such labor in collaboration with KSA and
the labor unions and prepare a plan for improvement in labor supply and
employment conditions, acceptable to the Bank/IDA, before December 31, 1979.
The plan would address the problems associated with working and living con-
ditions, job referral, transport and relocation, and compensation; specific
proposals would be prepared for Ramisi. In an effort to provide immediate
relief, the Project would improve staff housing in all zones (Annex 10).



- 163 -
ANNEX 13
Page 8
28.       The National Sugar Research Institute would be strengthened through
provision of additional staff and staff training. Three experts, one each
in the fields of plant breeding (four years), plant physiology and sugarcane
agronomy (three years), would be internationally recruited should suitable
local candidates be unavailable (Annex 6). The expanded functions of the
Kenya Sugar Authority would be undertaken with a combination of additional
staff employed by KSA and consultants whose terms and conditions of appoint-
ment would have to be acceptable to the Bank/IDA.
E.  Project Construction OrRanization
29.       The irrigation schemes at Miwani, Chemelil, Muhoroni and Ramisi
would be implemented by consultants appointed and supervised by KSA. The
National Irrigation Board (NIB) would be responsible for the conversion of the
Ahero scheme and, after construction, for operational supervision of all
irrigation schemes. All works would be maintained by the respective owners
and, at Ahero, the NIB (Annex 4). The drainage component would be implemented
by consultants appointed and supervised by KSA (Annex 5). The sugar companies
would be responsible for agricultural development (Annex 6), factory improve-
ments (Annex 9), housing and social infrastructure (Annex 10), upgrading of
existing and construction of new unclassified roads and tracks in outgrower
areas (Annex 12), and company research and training. The companies also would
maintain the drainage and roads infrastructure for the Government (para. 15).
30.       The Ministry of Works would be responsible for the construction
of new and upgrading of existing classified roads in all areas and of un-
classified roads in large-farm areas and for the construction of bridges
and other major structures; it also would supervise the maintenance of roads by
the sugar companies (Annex 10). The Ministry of Agriculture would undertake to
strengthen the National Sugar Research Institute; in particular, it would
reopen the cane breeding station at Mtwapa and add a training center at Kibos
(Annex 6). The Kenya Sugar Authority would be the executing agency for the
four required studies, conduct specified training activities (Annex 8), and
appoint consultants for the irrigation and drainage components and for the
review and approval of the companies' factory component, and establish and
operate an infrastructure maintenance fund (para. 15). The Chief Executive of
KSA would be chairman of the Sugar Belt Coordinating Committee. The coopera-
tive unions and societies would assist in the implementation of the outgrowers
component (para. 8). The Sugar Settlement Organization would prepare a plan
for the reorganization of the Sugar Settlement Scheme and be responsible for
its implementation (para. 12).
January 20, 1978

ANNEX 15
Appendix l
Page 1
KENYA
SUGAR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Financial Assumptions
1.        Revenues and expenses are stated in a constant monetary unit (1978).
2.        The project is to be implemented from 1979 to 1983.  Investments
required to replace the capital equipment beyond 1983 are included
in the projections.
3.        Sales volumes and prices for the four companies are listed on
Table 26.
4.        Sales include net revenue from sale of molasses.
5.        Variable costs include the cost of cane purchased from outgrowers,
nucleus estate production cost, and variable factory costs. The
selling price of cane and estimated recovery rates are in Table 26.
Nucleus estate production costs and factory costs are calculated
in Annex 11. Variable costs decrease from 1979 to 1981 because of
productivity gains resulting from investments made by the companies
before the Project.
6.        Fixed expenses include factory overhead, general administration,
and depreciation charges. Depreciation was provided for substan-
tially all equipment on a straight line basis according to expected
useful lives of individual items. Fixed expenses of Chemelil and
Muhoroni also included a management fee.
7.        Interest on the Rehabilitation subloans was provided at 10.5% p.a.;
on other loans, the actual rate was applied.
8.        The corporate income tax rate of 45% was calculated where appli-
cable. Some of the companies have benefited or are expected to
benefit in the future from the provisions of Kenya Tax code regard-
ing tax loss carry-forward.
9.        Incremental dividends were projected for Chemelil only, beginning
in 1979, as recommended by Chemelil management.
lO.       Current assets and liabilities have been projected according to
estimated production from Table 26 and growing cycles, as illus-
trated in Annex 6, Tables 1-5.
11.       Net trading assets equal current assets minus cash, minus current
liabilities.



- 211 -
ANNEX 15
Appendix 1
Page 2
12.       Rehabilitation subloans are to be drawn down in 5 years, have a
final maturity of 20 years and are repaid in 15 years beginning the
last day of drawdown period. Ramisi would receive three year grace
period and repay in 12 years.
13.       Muhoroni will receive KSh. 24 million additional equity financing;
Ramisi, Ksh. 36 million.
